Resolving Doubt: The Hanafi Rule of Acting on the Minimum and the Role of Precaution
Six branches of doubt: impurity, debt, zakat, fasts, iddah, vows, oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of acting on the lesser certainty (the minimum) when in doubt, and identify when precaution (ihtiyat) overrides the minimum, based on the six furu' presented.

Core Idea
When doubt cannot be resolved, act on the lesser certainty unless precaution is required by a stronger proof.

This passage from Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir presents six practical branches (furu') where a person faces doubt about a religious obligation or status. The Hanafi school generally resolves such doubt by adhering to the principle of certainty (yaqin) and taking the minimum necessary, unless there is a specific reason to adopt the precautionary (ihtiyat) position. As a mufti, you will often encounter similar cases where you must decide between the minimum and the cautious ruling. This lesson walks through each branch and its underlying logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of doubt
For each branch, specify what is uncertain (e.g., discharge type, amount owed, exact vow).
The doubt may be about the nature of an act, quantity, or condition.
2
Determine the minimum certain obligation
What is the least that must be done to fulfill the duty? For example, if uncertain between semen and madhi, the minimum is madhi (only wudu).
This step applies the maxim: 'Certainty is not removed by doubt.'
3
Check if precaution overrides the minimum
Some imams (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) may require the greater act (ghusl) as a precaution, especially by analogy to similar cases.
Precaution is applied when there is a strong indication or to avoid missing a wajib.
4
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling) for each branch
Consider the authority level (zahir al-riwayah vs. later fatwa) and the general trend in the school.
Later Hanafi texts often favour precaution in doubtful purity matters.
5
Apply the same method to new cases
Use this pattern to resolve any doubt-based question: identify the minimum, then see if precaution is warranted by a supporting analogy or consensus.
The six branches are a model, not an exhaustive list.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And among them: Doubt about discharge: semen or madhi, and it was during sleep: if he remembers a wet dream, ghusl is obligatory by consensus; otherwise, it is not obligatory according to Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) acting on the minimum which is madhi, and it is obligatory according to them (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) as a precaution, like their statement regarding invalidation by shameless touching, and like the Imam's statement regarding the dead mouse if found in a well and it is not known when it fell. The text also discusses the rule of 'predominant opinion' in oath-taking. The later addition from al-Yatima clarifies a conditional oath scenario. The Maxim Al-Asl Al-Adam: Default is Non-Existence
Collection of examples applying the maxim al-Asl al-Adam

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim al-asl al-adam to resolve disputes in various fiqh chapters, distinguishing between accidental and original attributes.

Core Idea
In disputes, the default is non-existence for accidental attributes, but existence for original attributes.

The Hanafi legal tradition relies on presumptions to resolve disputes when evidence is lacking. One of the most frequently invoked maxims is al-asl al-adam — the default assumption is non-existence. This lesson examines this maxim through a series of practical furuat (derived cases) drawn from classical Hanafi texts. You will learn not only the maxim itself but also its critical limitation: it applies only to accidental attributes, not original ones.

Guided Path
1
Understand the maxim and its scope
The maxim al-asl al-adam means that in the absence of proof, we assume a state or attribute did not exist. It is used to allocate the burden of proof in disputes.
This is a wusta (intermediate) maxim, governing many fiqh chapters.
2
Identify the dabt (restriction)
The maxim is not absolute. The text adds a crucial restriction: it applies only to accidental attributes (al-sifat al-arida) such as profession or skill. For original attributes (al-sifat al-asliyya) like virginity, the default is existence.
The sub-maxim: al-asl al-adam fi al-sifat al-arida, wa al-asl al-wujud fi al-sifat al-asliyya.
3
Work through the furuat (applications)
The passage lists multiple cases. For each, identify which party bears the burden of proof and why the default assumption favours them.
Example: If a creditor claims non-payment, the default is non-existence of payment, so the creditor's word is taken.
4
Apply the distinction
For each furu, classify the disputed attribute as accidental or original. Confirm that the ruling aligns with the appropriate default.
Baking is accidental (default non-existence); virginity is original (default existence).
5
Summarise the takeaway
When facing a dispute, first determine whether the contested matter is an accidental or original attribute. Then apply the correct default presumption to assign the burden of proof.
This nuanced approach is a hallmark of Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Commentary
This passage presents numerous applications of the Hanafi legal maxim 'al-asl al-‘adam' – the default assumption is non-existence. The maxim is a key interpretive tool in matters of dispute. The author lists case after case: if someone claims a deposit was taken as a usurious loan, the default is no liability; if the parties dispute whether a transfer was a loan or a gift, the giver's word is taken because a gift requires a discharge from liability; if a nursing mother doesn't know if milk entered the child's throat, marriage is not prohibited because the default is no impediment. In disputes about delivery of goods, the denier's word holds; if a creditor claims repayment, the creditor's word stands because the original is that the debt remains unpaid. For defects, the seller's denial is accepted unless proven otherwise, though there is a difference of opinion on the reasoning. For option stipulations, the preferred view is that the party denying the option is believed. In usurpation and profit disputes, the owner's claim is taken. For seeing the object, the buyer's claim is believed; for changes after seeing, the seller's claim is believed. A crucial nuance is provided in the 'note': this maxim applies only to accidental attributes; for original attributes (like virginity), the default is existence. Thus if a buyer claims the seller is not a baker, the buyer's word is taken; but if a buyer claims the sold slave girl is not a virgin, the seller's word is taken because virginity is an original attribute.

Key Terms
الأصل العدم (al-asl al-‘adam): Presumption that a state or attribute did not exist unless proven otherwise
الصفات العارضة (al-sifat al-‘arida): Accidental attributes like profession, which are presumed absent until proven
الصفات الأصلية (al-sifat al-asliyya): Inherent attributes like virginity, presumed present until proven otherwise

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an accidental attribute according to the passage?
A) Virginity (bikarah)
B) Life (hayat)
C) Being a baker (khubaz)
D) Being a human (insaniyya)

Key Takeaway
The maxim al-asl al-adam is a powerful tool for allocating burden of proof, but it is restricted to accidental attributes; original attributes default to existence.

The Maxim: Attributing Events to Their Nearest Time
Analysis of a major Hanafi maxim with furu' and istithna'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-asl idafat al-hadith ila aqrab awqatihi' to cases of unknown timing and identify its exception in financial matters.

Core Idea
When an event's timing is unknown, attribute it to the nearest plausible moment, unless financial interests dictate otherwise.

This lesson introduces a key Hanafi legal maxim that governs cases where the timing of an event is unknown. The maxim presumes that an event occurred at the nearest possible time, unless there is a reason to act differently. We will explore its applications in purity, disputes, and inheritance, and understand when precaution or financial considerations override it.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its rationale
Clearly articulate the maxim: 'Al-asl idafat al-hadith ila aqrab awqatihi' - the default is to assign an event to its nearest plausible time. This is based on the principle that certainty is not overridden by doubt.
The maxim applies when the exact time of an event is unknown.
2
Apply the maxim to impurity on clothes
Work through the first example: a person finds impurity on their garment after praying. Since the time of impurity is unknown, the prayer is repeated from the last known ritual impurity (e.g., last sleep for semen, last urination for urine, last nosebleed for blood).
This is a direct application of the maxim.
3
Analyze the dead mouse in a garment
Examine the second example: a dead mouse is found in a garment. The ruling depends on whether there is a hole. Without a hole, the mouse could only have entered when the garment was stuffed with cotton, so prayers are repeated from that day. With a hole, it could have entered anytime up to three days prior, so prayers are repeated from three days.
The presence of a hole expands the possible timeframe.
4
Compare the positions on well contamination
The two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) apply the maxim strictly: when a dead mouse is found in a well, it is considered impure only from the moment of discovery, so no past prayers are repeated. Imam Abu Hanifa disagrees, using istihsan (juristic preference) to require repetition of prayers for up to three days based on the apparent cause, as a precaution.
This illustrates a intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf driven by different weighting of the maxim versus precaution.
5
Apply the maxim to disputes and note the exception
In disputes over who damaged a slave, or whether a divorce occurred in illness or health, the maxim supports the claimant who alleges the more recent event. However, in the case of a dhimmi's wife claiming apostasy after his death (to inherit), the maxim would favor her, but the Hanafi school departs from it due to financial considerations: the cause of disinheritance (apostasy) is established in the present, so it is presumed for the past.
This exception shows that the maxim does not apply in financial matters when a present cause is apparent.

Rule: The default is to attribute an event to its nearest time. Among its examples is what we presented: If he saw impurity on his garment and prayed with it, and he does not know when it affected him, he repeats the prayer from the last impurity he had; and for semen, from the last sleep, he must perform ghusl for the second sleep according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, even if he does not recall a wet dream. In al-Bada’i‘, he repeats from the last wet dream, and it is said for urine he considers from the last time he urinated, and for blood from the last time he had a nosebleed. If he splits a garment and finds a dead mouse inside, and does not know when it entered; if there is no hole, he repeats prayer from the day he put the cotton in it; if there is a hole, he repeats from three days. The two shaykhs acted on this rule and ruled that if a dead mouse is found in a well, it is impure from the time of knowledge without repeating anything, because its fall is an event attributed to its nearest time. The Great Imam disagreed; he istihsan that he repeats prayer for three days if the mouse is swollen or rotten, otherwise from a day and a night, acting on the apparent cause rather than the conjectural, as a precaution, like a wounded person who remains bedridden until death, the cause is attributed to the wound. If he does not provide evidence and wants to make them swear, he may do so. (End). And from what I derived from this
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage introduces another key Hanafi maxim: 'al-asl idafat al-hadith ila aqrab awqatihi' – the default is to assign an event to the nearest possible time when its actual time is unknown. The author gives several applications. If a person finds impurity on his garment after prayers and doesn't know when it occurred, he repeats the prayers from the time of his last known impurity or event (like sleep, urination, nosebleed). For a dead mouse discovered inside a garment, repetition depends on whether there is a hole: if no hole, from the day of stuffing; if hole, from three days. The two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) applied the maxim to well contamination: if a dead mouse is found, the well becomes impure from the time of discovery only, without repeating past prayers. Imam Abu Hanifa differed, requiring repetition for three days or a day and night depending on the state of the mouse, acting on the apparent cause as a precaution. In disputes over an eye gouging, the buyer's claim is accepted. In inheritance, a wife claiming divorce during illness inherits. An exception is noted: if a dhimmi's wife claims she apostatized after his death, the heirs' claim is taken due to financial considerations. Another branch: in acknowledging a debt to an heir in fatal illness, the heirs' statement is accepted because acknowledgment in illness is treated as a bequest from one-third.

Key Terms
إضافة الحادث إلى أقرب أوقاته (idafat al-hadith ila aqrab awqatihi): Presumption that an event occurred at the nearest plausible time
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference over strict analogy

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) One day
C) Three days
D) Seven days

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-asl idafat al-hadith ila aqrab awqatihi' is a powerful tool for resolving timing doubts, but it is overridden in financial matters when a present cause is established.

The Maxim: Attributing Events to the Nearest Time
Rule statement with initial furūʿ from ṭahārah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'al-aṣl iḍāfah al-ḥādith ilā aqrab awqātih' and apply it to common purity scenarios such as impurity on a garment, semen, urine, and a dead mouse in a cloak.

Core Idea
When the time of an event is unknown, it is attributed to the nearest possible time.

The Hanafi school employs a powerful rule to resolve uncertainties about when an event occurred. This maxim governs cases from impurity on garments to the timing of a divorce. In this lesson, we will understand the rule itself and its application in several purity cases from the text.

Guided Path
1
Identify the event and its uncertainty
First, identify the event (e.g., impurity found, semen from sleep, mouse in cloak) and note that the exact time is unknown.
The event is the 'ḥādith' whose timing is uncertain.
2
Determine the 'nearest time' for attribution
Look for the most recent relevant event that could have caused the discovered state. For impurity on a garment, it is the last impurity event (urination, wet dream, nosebleed). For a mouse in a cloak with no hole, it is the day cotton was placed; with a hole, the last three days.
The 'aqrab waqt' is the latest possible time the event could have occurred.
3
Apply the maxim to derive the ruling
Attribute the event to that nearest time and determine the legal consequence. For impurity on a garment, repeat prayers from that time onward. For semen, perform ghusl for the second sleep even without memory.
The prayer repetition or ghusl obligation is based on the attributed time.

وتحتاج هذه المسائل إلى نظر دقيق للفرق بينها وفي المجمع من الإقرار ولو أسلم بأخذ المال قبل الإسلام أو بإتلاف خمر بعده، أو مسلم بمال حربي في دار الحرب أو بقطع يد معتقه قبل العتق ; فكذبوه في الإسناد. أفتى بعدم في الكل. (انتهى) يعني محمد، وقالا يضمن. ومما فرع عليه; لو اشترى عبدا ثم ظهر أنه كان مريضًا ومات عند المشتري ; فإنه لا يرجع بالثمن لأن المرض يتزايد فيحصل الموت بالزائد فلا يضاف إلى السابق، لكن يرجع بنقصان العيب كما ذكره الزيلعي. وليس من فروعها ما إذا تزوج أمة ثم اشتراها ثم ولدت ولدا، يحتمل أن يكون حادثا بعد الشراء أو قبله; فإنه لا شك عندنا في كونها أم ولد لا من جهة أنه حادث أُضيف إلى أقرب أوقاته ; لأنها لو ولدت قبل الشراء ثم ملكها تصير أُم ولده عندنا .
Rule: The principle is to attribute an event to its nearest time. From it is what we have presented: regarding someone who sees impurity on his garment and prayed in it and does not know when it struck him, he repeats the prayer from the last impurity he incurred; and for semen, from the last sleep, he must perform ghusl in the second sleep according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, even if he does not remember a wet dream. In al-Bada'i', he repeats from the last time he had a wet dream, and it is said for urine, consider the last time he urinated, and for blood, the last time he had a nosebleed. If one tears open a cloak and finds a dead mouse and does not know when it entered, if there is no hole, he repeats prayer from the day he placed cotton in it; if there is a hole, he repeats from three days. The two shaykhs acted on this rule and ruled the well impure if a dead mouse is found in it from the time of knowledge without repeating anything, because its occurrence is an event attributed to its nearest time. The Greatest Imam disagreed and considered it better to repeat prayer of three days if it is swollen or rotten, otherwise from a day and a night, acting on the apparent cause and not the imaginary, as a precaution, like a wounded person who did not leave his bed until he died, so it is attributed to the wound. Among its applications: if a man has a slave in his hand, and a man says, 'I gouged his eye' while it was in the seller's possession, and the buyer says, 'I gouged it while in my possession,' the buyer's word is taken and he takes the compensation. Also: if a woman claims her husband divorced her during illness and he became a fugitive, so she inherits; the heirs say he divorced her in health so she does not inherit; her word is taken and she inherits. Excluded from this principle is the issue of al-Kanz from various judicial issues: if a dhimmi dies and his wife says she converted after his death, and the heirs say she converted before his death, the heirs' word is taken, although the mentioned principle would require her word to be taken, and Zufar held this. They excluded from this rule for the sake of strengthening wealth, because the cause of deprivation is established in the present, so it is established in the past. Among what I derived from the principle is in al-Yatimah and others: if someone makes an acknowledgment to an heir and then dies, and the acknowledgee says the acknowledgment was in health, and the heirs say during illness, the heirs' word is taken, and the burden of proof is on the acknowledgee. If he does not provide proof and wants to administer an oath to them, he may do so. (End). Among what is derived from this principle is their statement: if a Muslim dies with a Christian wife, and a Muslim woman comes after his death and says she converted before his death, and the heirs say she converted after his death, the heirs' word is taken, as al-Zayla'i mentioned in various issues. Excluded from this principle: if a judge after removal says to a man, 'I took a thousand from you and paid it to Zaid as a judgment against you,' and the man says, 'You took it unjustly after removal,' the correct view is that the judge's word is taken, although the act is an event that should be attributed to its nearest time, which is the time of removal; some held this and al-Sarakhsi chose it, but the relied-upon is the first because the judge attributed it to a state inconsistent with liability. Similarly, if the one from whom it was taken claims he did it before the appointment of the judge. Also excluded from it is if a slave after emancipation says to another, 'I cut your hand while I was a slave,' and the acknowledgee says, 'You cut it while you were free,' the slave's word is taken. Similarly, if a master says to his slave after emancipating him, 'I took from you five dirhams monthly while you were a slave,' and the freed slave says, 'You took it after emancipation,' the master's word is taken. Similarly, an agent for sale: if he says, 'I sold and delivered before removal' and the principal says 'after removal,' the agent's word is taken if the sold item is consumed; if it remains, the principal's word is taken. Similarly, in the issue of produce, he is not believed regarding produce that remains. What agrees with the principle is in al-Nihayah: if a man emancipates a slave woman and then says, 'I cut your hand while you were my slave,' and she says, 'You cut it while I was free,' her word is taken. Similarly, for everything taken from her according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, mentioned before the chapter on testimonies. Acknowledgment of a Harbi, 6, al-Daman. These issues require precise consideration of the difference between them. In al-Majma' from the chapter on acknowledgment: if a Muslim acknowledges taking wealth before Islam or destroying wine after it, or a Muslim acknowledges wealth of a Harbi in dar al-harb, or acknowledging cutting off the hand of his freed slave before emancipation, and they deny the attribution, Muhammad gave a fatwa of non-liability in all. (End) meaning Muhammad, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said he is liable. Among what is derived from it: if someone buys a slave then it appears he was sick and dies with the buyer, he does not recover the price because illness increases and death occurs from the increase, so it is not attributed to the previous, but he recovers for the defect as al-Zayla'i mentioned. Not from its branches is if someone marries a slave woman, then buys her, then she gives birth, and it is possible the birth occurred after purchase or before; there is no doubt in our school that she becomes umm walad, not because the event is attributed to its nearest time, because if she gave birth before purchase then he owned her, she becomes umm walad in our school.

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi legal maxim 'Events are attributed to the nearest possible time' (الأصل إضافة الحادث إلى أقرب أوقاته). The author begins by stating the maxim and then provides numerous practical applications from various chapters of fiqh, illustrating how the rule operates. The first example involves impurity on a garment discovered after prayer: the prayer is repeated from the last time the person had an impurity event, not from an earlier time. Similar reasoning applies to semen requiring ghusl from the last sleep, urine from the last urination, and blood from the last nosebleed. The text then discusses a case of a dead mouse found in a cloak: if there is no hole, prayer is repeated from the day cotton was placed; if there is a hole (through which the mouse could enter), prayer is repeated from three days. The two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) applied the maxim to a well: if a dead mouse is found, the well water becomes impure from the time of knowledge without requiring re-prayer of past days. However, Imam Abu Hanifa disagreed and ruled based on apparent signs (swelling/rottenness) to determine the time, requiring prayer repetition for three days or one day/night. This shows an internal Hanafi ikhtilaf based on preference for apparent cause over pure attribution. Further applications include disputes over eye-gouging of a slave (buyer's word taken if ownership is contested), and a wife's claim of divorce during illness (her word taken for inheritance). The text then lists exceptions to the maxim: in the case of a dhimmi's wife converting after his death, the heirs' word is taken (contrary to the maxim) to protect financial interests (the wife's inheritance is prevented). Another exception involves a judge after removal: his word is taken regarding payment he made, because his role as judge gives his statement weight. Further exceptions involve slaves after emancipation and agents. The passage concludes with related issues from al-Majma' about acknowledgments of wartime acts and a discussion of a sick slave purchased who dies: the buyer cannot recover the price because death is attributed to the increasing illness, not the pre-existing condition. Finally, the case of a slave woman who gives birth after purchase: she becomes umm walad regardless, not because of the maxim but because ownership after birth establishes the status. The text emphasizes that careful distinction is needed between these cases.

Key Terms
الأصل إضافة الحادث إلى أقرب أوقاته (al-aṣl iḍāfah al-ḥādith ilā aqrab awqātih): A legal maxim stating that when the time of an event is unknown, it is attributed to the nearest possible time (the most recent event that could have caused it).
الحدث (al-ḥadath): An event; here specifically a ritual impurity (hadath) that necessitates wudu or ghusl.
الغسل (al-ghusl): Full-body ritual washing required in cases of major impurity (janabah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, if a man finds semen on his garment after sleeping twice, and he does not remember a wet dream, from which sleep must he perform ghusl?
A) The first sleep
B) The second sleep
C) Both sleeps
D) He does not need ghusl because he does not remember

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'attribution to the nearest time' resolves uncertainty by linking events to their most recent possible cause.

Internal Hanafi Ikhtilaf: The Well and the Dead Mouse
Internal dispute within the Hanafi school over application of the maxim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Imam Abu Hanifa's and Shaykhayn's application of the maxim in the well case, and identify the role of istihsan.

Core Idea
Imam Abu Hanifa prefers acting on apparent signs (istihsan) over strict attribution to the nearest time.

The maxim of attribution to the nearest time is not applied mechanically. In the case of a dead mouse found in a well, the two leading students of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, applied it strictly, while Abu Hanifa himself used istihsan based on visible signs. This lesson examines that ikhtilaf and its practical implications for the mufti.

Guided Path
1
State the factual scenario
A dead mouse is found in a well; the time of its entry is unknown. The question is: from when does the water become impure and must past prayers be repeated?
The well water is used for drinking and wudu.
2
Apply the maxim strictly (Shaykhayn's view)
Attributing the event to its nearest time means the mouse fell in at the latest possible time, which is the time of discovery (time of knowledge). Therefore, impurity starts from that moment, and no past prayers are invalidated.
This is a literal application: 'because its occurrence is an event attributed to its nearest time.'
3
Apply istihsan (Abu Hanifa's view)
Abu Hanifa looks at the apparent signs: if the mouse is swollen or rotten, it must have been dead for at least three days; otherwise, one day. He acts on this cause (sabab zahir) as a precaution, requiring prayer repetition from that estimated time.
Istihsan here overrides the strict maxim because visible evidence provides a more reliable inference.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf and tarjih
The practical difference: under Shaykhayn, no prayers are repeated; under Abu Hanifa, prayers from three days (or one day) are repeated. The mufti may choose either view based on circumstances.
Later Hanafi practice often leans toward Shaykhayn for ease, but Abu Hanifa's istihsan is also valid.

Source Text

قاعدة الأصل إضافة الحادث إلى أقرب أوقاته منها ما قدمناه؛ فيما لو رأى في ثوبه نجاسة وقد صلَّى فيه ولا يدري متى أصابته يعيدها من آخر حدث أحدثه، والمني من آخر رقدة يلزمه الغسل في الثانية عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله وإن لم يتذكر احتلامًا. وفي البدائع يعيد من آخر ما احتلم وقيل في البول يعتبر من آخر ما بال، وفي الدم من آخر ما رعف ولو فتق جبة فوجد فيها فأرة ميتة ولم يعلم متى دخلت فيها؛ فإن لم يكن لها ثقب يُعيد الصلاة مُنْ يوم وضع القطن فيها، وإن كان فيها ثقب يعيدها من ثلاثة أيام. وقد عمل الشيخان بهذه القاعدة فحكما بنجاسة البئر إذا وجدت فيها فأرة ميتة من وقت العلم بها من غير إعادة شيء، لأن وقوعها حادث فيضاف إلى أقرب أوقاته وخالف الإمام الأعظم رحمه الله ؛ فاستحسن إعادة صلاة ثلاثة أيام إن كانت منتفخة أو متفسخة وإلا فمنذ يوم وليلة عملاً بالسبب الظاهر دون الموهوم احتياطا، كالمجروح إذا لم يزل صاحب فراش حتى مات يُحال به على الجرح. ومنها: لو كان في يد رجل عبد؛ فقال رجل : فقأت عينه، وهو في ملك البائع، وقال المشتري فقأته وهو في ملكي. فالقول للمشتري فيأخذ أرشه. : ومنها : ادعت أن زوجها أبانها في المرض وصار فارا، فترث. وقالت الورثة أبانها في صحته فلا ترث. كان القول قولها فترث.
وخرج عن هذا الأصل مسألة الكنز من مسائل شتى من القضاء. وإن مات ذمي فقالت زوجته أسلمت بعد موته وقالت الورثة أسلمت قبل موته. فالقول لهم مع أن الأصل المذكور يقتضي أن يكون القول قولها وبه قال زفر رحمه الله تعالى. وإنما خرجوا عن هذه القاعدة فيها لأجل تحكيم المال وهو أن سبب الحرمان ثابت في الحال فيثبت فيما مضى.
ومما فرعته على الأصل ما في اليتيمة وغيرها ولو أقر لوارث ثم مات فقال المقرّ له أقرّ في الصحة. وقالت الورثة في مرضه ; فالقول قول الورثة والبينة بينة المقرّ له. وإن لم يقم ببينته وأراد استحلافهم فله ذلك .(انتهى). ومما فرعته على هذا الأصل قولهم ; لو مات مسلم وتحته نصرانية فجاءت مسلمة بعد موته، وقالت أسلمت قبل موته وقالت الورثة أسلمت بعد موته ; فالقول لهم كما ذكره الزيلعي في مسائل شتى. ومما خرج عن هذا الأصل; لو قال القاضي بعد عزله لرجل أخذت منك ألفًا ودفعتها إلى زيد قضيت بها عليك فقال الرجل أخذتها ظلمًا بعد العزل; فالصحيح أن القول للقاضي مع أن الفعل حادث، فكان ينبغي أن يضاف إلى أقرب أوقاته وهو وقت العزل، وبه قال البعض واختاره السرخسي. لكن المعتمد الأول لأن القاضي أسنده إلى حالة منافية للضمان. وكذلك إذا زعم المأخوذ منه أنه فعله قبل تقليد القضاء. وخرج أيضًا عنه ما لو قال العبد لغيره بعد العتق قطعت يدك وأنا عبد. وقال المقرّ له بل قطعتها وأنت حرّ، كان القول للعبد وكذا لو قال المولى لعبده، وقد أعتقه، أخذت منك غلة كل شهر خمسة دراهم وأنت عبد ، فقال المعتق أخذتها بعد العتق، كان القول قول المولى. وكذا الوكيل بالبيع; إذا قال بعت وسلمت قبل العزل وقال الموكل بعد العزل كان القول للوكيل إن كان المبيع مستهلكا، وإن كان قائما فالقول قول الموكل. وكذا في مسألة الغلة لا يصدق في الغلة القائمة. ومما وافق الأصل ما في النهاية : لو أعتق أمة ثم قال لها قطعت يدك وأنت أمتي، فقالت هي قطعتها وأنا حرة فالقول قولها. وكذا في كل شيء أخذه منها عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف رحمه الله ، ذكره قبيل الشهادات .

الضمان
وتحتاج هذه المسائل إلى نظر دقيق للفرق بينها وفي المجمع من الإقرار ولو أسلم بأخذ المال قبل الإسلام أو بإتلاف خمر بعده، أو مسلم بمال حربي في دار الحرب أو بقطع يد معتقه قبل العتق ; فكذبوه في الإسناد. أفتى بعدم في الكل. (انتهى) يعني محمد، وقالا يضمن. ومما فرع عليه; لو اشترى عبدا ثم ظهر أنه كان مريضًا ومات عند المشتري ; فإنه لا يرجع بالثمن لأن المرض يتزايد فيحصل الموت بالزائد فلا يضاف إلى السابق، لكن يرجع بنقصان العيب كما ذكره الزيلعي. وليس من فروعها ما إذا تزوج أمة ثم اشتراها ثم ولدت ولدا، يحتمل أن يكون حادثا بعد الشراء أو قبله; فإنه لا شك عندنا في كونها أم ولد لا من جهة أنه حادث أُضيف إلى أقرب أوقاته ; لأنها لو ولدت قبل الشراء ثم ملكها تصير أُم ولده عندنا .
Rule: The principle is to attribute an event to its nearest time. From it is what we have presented: regarding someone who sees impurity on his garment and prayed in it and does not know when it struck him, he repeats the prayer from the last impurity he incurred; and for semen, from the last sleep, he must perform ghusl in the second sleep according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, even if he does not remember a wet dream. In al-Bada'i', he repeats from the last time he had a wet dream, and it is said for urine, consider the last time he urinated, and for blood, the last time he had a nosebleed. If one tears open a cloak and finds a dead mouse and does not know when it entered, if there is no hole, he repeats prayer from the day he placed cotton in it; if there is a hole, he repeats from three days. The two shaykhs acted on this rule and ruled the well impure if a dead mouse is found in it from the time of knowledge without repeating anything, because its occurrence is an event attributed to its nearest time. The Greatest Imam disagreed and considered it better to repeat prayer of three days if it is swollen or rotten, otherwise from a day and a night, acting on the apparent cause and not the imaginary, as a precaution, like a wounded person who did not leave his bed until he died, so it is attributed to the wound. Among its applications: if a man has a slave in his hand, and a man says, 'I gouged his eye' while it was in the seller's possession, and the buyer says, 'I gouged it while in my possession,' the buyer's word is taken and he takes the compensation. Also: if a woman claims her husband divorced her during illness and he became a fugitive, so she inherits; the heirs say he divorced her in health so she does not inherit; her word is taken and she inherits. Excluded from this principle is the issue of al-Kanz from various judicial issues: if a dhimmi dies and his wife says she converted after his death, and the heirs say she converted before his death, the heirs' word is taken, although the mentioned principle would require her word to be taken, and Zufar held this. They excluded from this rule for the sake of strengthening wealth, because the cause of deprivation is established in the present, so it is established in the past. Among what I derived from the principle is in al-Yatimah and others: if someone makes an acknowledgment to an heir and then dies, and the acknowledgee says the acknowledgment was in health, and the heirs say during illness, the heirs' word is taken, and the burden of proof is on the acknowledgee. If he does not provide proof and wants to administer an oath to them, he may do so. (End). Among what is derived from this principle is their statement: if a Muslim dies with a Christian wife, and a Muslim woman comes after his death and says she converted before his death, and the heirs say she converted after his death, the heirs' word is taken, as al-Zayla'i mentioned in various issues. Excluded from this principle: if a judge after removal says to a man, 'I took a thousand from you and paid it to Zaid as a judgment against you,' and the man says, 'You took it unjustly after removal,' the correct view is that the judge's word is taken, although the act is an event that should be attributed to its nearest time, which is the time of removal; some held this and al-Sarakhsi chose it, but the relied-upon is the first because the judge attributed it to a state inconsistent with liability. Similarly, if the one from whom it was taken claims he did it before the appointment of the judge. Also excluded from it is if a slave after emancipation says to another, 'I cut your hand while I was a slave,' and the acknowledgee says, 'You cut it while you were free,' the slave's word is taken. Similarly, if a master says to his slave after emancipating him, 'I took from you five dirhams monthly while you were a slave,' and the freed slave says, 'You took it after emancipation,' the master's word is taken. Similarly, an agent for sale: if he says, 'I sold and delivered before removal' and the principal says 'after removal,' the agent's word is taken if the sold item is consumed; if it remains, the principal's word is taken. Similarly, in the issue of produce, he is not believed regarding produce that remains. What agrees with the principle is in al-Nihayah: if a man emancipates a slave woman and then says, 'I cut your hand while you were my slave,' and she says, 'You cut it while I was free,' her word is taken. Similarly, for everything taken from her according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, mentioned before the chapter on testimonies. Acknowledgment of a Harbi, 6, al-Daman. These issues require precise consideration of the difference between them. In al-Majma' from the chapter on acknowledgment: if a Muslim acknowledges taking wealth before Islam or destroying wine after it, or a Muslim acknowledges wealth of a Harbi in dar al-harb, or acknowledging cutting off the hand of his freed slave before emancipation, and they deny the attribution, Muhammad gave a fatwa of non-liability in all. (End) meaning Muhammad, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said he is liable. Among what is derived from it: if someone buys a slave then it appears he was sick and dies with the buyer, he does not recover the price because illness increases and death occurs from the increase, so it is not attributed to the previous, but he recovers for the defect as al-Zayla'i mentioned. Not from its branches is if someone marries a slave woman, then buys her, then she gives birth, and it is possible the birth occurred after purchase or before; there is no doubt in our school that she becomes umm walad, not because the event is attributed to its nearest time, because if she gave birth before purchase then he owned her, she becomes umm walad in our school.

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi legal maxim 'Events are attributed to the nearest possible time' (الأصل إضافة الحادث إلى أقرب أوقاته). The author begins by stating the maxim and then provides numerous practical applications from various chapters of fiqh, illustrating how the rule operates. The first example involves impurity on a garment discovered after prayer: the prayer is repeated from the last time the person had an impurity event, not from an earlier time. Similar reasoning applies to semen requiring ghusl from the last sleep, urine from the last urination, and blood from the last nosebleed. The text then discusses a case of a dead mouse found in a cloak: if there is no hole, prayer is repeated from the day cotton was placed; if there is a hole (through which the mouse could enter), prayer is repeated from three days. The two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) applied the maxim to a well: if a dead mouse is found, the well water becomes impure from the time of knowledge without requiring re-prayer of past days. However, Imam Abu Hanifa disagreed and ruled based on apparent signs (swelling/rottenness) to determine the time, requiring prayer repetition for three days or one day/night. This shows an internal Hanafi ikhtilaf based on preference for apparent cause over pure attribution. Further applications include disputes over eye-gouging of a slave (buyer's word taken if ownership is contested), and a wife's claim of divorce during illness (her word taken for inheritance). The text then lists exceptions to the maxim: in the case of a dhimmi's wife converting after his death, the heirs' word is taken (contrary to the maxim) to protect financial interests (the wife's inheritance is prevented). Another exception involves a judge after removal: his word is taken regarding payment he made, because his role as judge gives his statement weight. Further exceptions involve slaves after emancipation and agents. The passage concludes with related issues from al-Majma' about acknowledgments of wartime acts and a discussion of a sick slave purchased who dies: the buyer cannot recover the price because death is attributed to the increasing illness, not the pre-existing condition. Finally, the case of a slave woman who gives birth after purchase: she becomes umm walad regardless, not because of the maxim but because ownership after birth establishes the status. The text emphasizes that careful distinction is needed between these cases.

Key Terms
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): Juristic preference; a Hanafi method of overriding a strict analogical ruling in favor of a more equitable or practical outcome.
الشيخان (al-shaykhān): The two shaykhs: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, the leading students of Abu Hanifa.
الإمام الأعظم (al-imām al-aʿẓam): The Greatest Imam, referring to Abu Hanifa.

Checkpoint
According to the Shaykhayn, from when does the well water become impure when a dead mouse is found?
A) From three days before discovery
B) From the time of discovery
C) From the last time the well was cleaned

Key Takeaway
The well case shows that the same maxim can lead to different rulings when jurists differ on whether to consider external evidence (istihsan) or apply the maxim strictly.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate
10 min
Applications and Exceptions of the Maxim
Expansion of the maxim to non-purity cases and its exceptions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim to disputes over slave eye-gouging and divorce timing, and identify its major exceptions (dhimmi wife, judge, slave, agent).

Core Idea
The maxim has broad application but is overridden by other principles in specific cases, particularly when financial interests or official authority are at stake.

Beyond purity, the maxim governs disputes over the timing of events like eye-gouging and divorce. However, Hanafi jurists identified several exceptions where the opposite ruling is given. This lesson surveys those applications and the rationale for the exceptions, preparing the mufti to recognize when to depart from the rule.

Guided Path
1
Apply the maxim to the slave eye-gouging dispute
A buyer and seller dispute when the slave's eye was gouged. The maxim says attribute the event (injury) to its nearest time, which is the later time (buyer's possession). Thus, the buyer's word is taken, and he receives compensation.
The buyer is the current possessor; the nearest event is during his ownership.
2
Apply the maxim to the wife's divorce claim
A wife claims divorce during illness, heirs claim during health. The maxim attributes the event (divorce) to its nearest time, which is the illness (later). Thus, the wife's word is taken, and she inherits.
This protects the wife's right to inheritance.
3
Identify the first exception: dhimmi wife conversion
A dhimmi dies, his widow claims she converted after his death (so she inherits as Muslim), but heirs claim before (so she does not). The maxim would favor her (nearest time = after death), but the exception overrides to protect the heirs' financial interest (she would inherit if she was Muslim at death).
Reason: the cause of deprivation (conversion after death) is established now, so it is attributed to the past.
4
Identify the second exception: judge after removal
A judge after removal claims he paid a sum; the defendant claims it was after removal (so unjust). The maxim would attribute to the nearest time (after removal), but the judge's word is taken because his role as judge gives his statement weight (act of judge is presumed valid).
Similar logic applies to the agent who sold and delivered before removal vs. after.
5
Identify the third exception: slave after emancipation
A slave says he injured someone while slave; the plaintiff says while free. The maxim would attribute to the nearest time (slave status, i.e., before emancipation), but the slave's word is taken because the claimant denies, and the default is to attribute to the nearest time (slave status) which here favors the slave.
This is an interesting case where the maxim itself leads to the opposite result due to the dynamics of denial.

Source Text

قاعدة الأصل إضافة الحادث إلى أقرب أوقاته منها ما قدمناه؛ فيما لو رأى في ثوبه نجاسة وقد صلَّى فيه ولا يدري متى أصابته يعيدها من آخر حدث أحدثه، والمني من آخر رقدة يلزمه الغسل في الثانية عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله وإن لم يتذكر احتلامًا. وفي البدائع يعيد من آخر ما احتلم وقيل في البول يعتبر من آخر ما بال، وفي الدم من آخر ما رعف ولو فتق جبة فوجد فيها فأرة ميتة ولم يعلم متى دخلت فيها؛ فإن لم يكن لها ثقب يُعيد الصلاة مُنْ يوم وضع القطن فيها، وإن كان فيها ثقب يعيدها من ثلاثة أيام. وقد عمل الشيخان بهذه القاعدة فحكما بنجاسة البئر إذا وجدت فيها فأرة ميتة من وقت العلم بها من غير إعادة شيء، لأن وقوعها حادث فيضاف إلى أقرب أوقاته وخالف الإمام الأعظم رحمه الله ؛ فاستحسن إعادة صلاة ثلاثة أيام إن كانت منتفخة أو متفسخة وإلا فمنذ يوم وليلة عملاً بالسبب الظاهر دون الموهوم احتياطا، كالمجروح إذا لم يزل صاحب فراش حتى مات يُحال به على الجرح. ومنها: لو كان في يد رجل عبد؛ فقال رجل : فقأت عينه، وهو في ملك البائع، وقال المشتري فقأته وهو في ملكي. فالقول للمشتري فيأخذ أرشه. : ومنها : ادعت أن زوجها أبانها في المرض وصار فارا، فترث. وقالت الورثة أبانها في صحته فلا ترث. كان القول قولها فترث.
وخرج عن هذا الأصل مسألة الكنز من مسائل شتى من القضاء. وإن مات ذمي فقالت زوجته أسلمت بعد موته وقالت الورثة أسلمت قبل موته. فالقول لهم مع أن الأصل المذكور يقتضي أن يكون القول قولها وبه قال زفر رحمه الله تعالى. وإنما خرجوا عن هذه القاعدة فيها لأجل تحكيم المال وهو أن سبب الحرمان ثابت في الحال فيثبت فيما مضى.
ومما فرعته على الأصل ما في اليتيمة وغيرها ولو أقر لوارث ثم مات فقال المقرّ له أقرّ في الصحة. وقالت الورثة في مرضه ; فالقول قول الورثة والبينة بينة المقرّ له. وإن لم يقم ببينته وأراد استحلافهم فله ذلك .(انتهى). ومما فرعته على هذا الأصل قولهم ; لو مات مسلم وتحته نصرانية فجاءت مسلمة بعد موته، وقالت أسلمت قبل موته وقالت الورثة أسلمت بعد موته ; فالقول لهم كما ذكره الزيلعي في مسائل شتى. ومما خرج عن هذا الأصل; لو قال القاضي بعد عزله لرجل أخذت منك ألفًا ودفعتها إلى زيد قضيت بها عليك فقال الرجل أخذتها ظلمًا بعد العزل; فالصحيح أن القول للقاضي مع أن الفعل حادث، فكان ينبغي أن يضاف إلى أقرب أوقاته وهو وقت العزل، وبه قال البعض واختاره السرخسي. لكن المعتمد الأول لأن القاضي أسنده إلى حالة منافية للضمان. وكذلك إذا زعم المأخوذ منه أنه فعله قبل تقليد القضاء. وخرج أيضًا عنه ما لو قال العبد لغيره بعد العتق قطعت يدك وأنا عبد. وقال المقرّ له بل قطعتها وأنت حرّ، كان القول للعبد وكذا لو قال المولى لعبده، وقد أعتقه، أخذت منك غلة كل شهر خمسة دراهم وأنت عبد ، فقال المعتق أخذتها بعد العتق، كان القول قول المولى. وكذا الوكيل بالبيع; إذا قال بعت وسلمت قبل العزل وقال الموكل بعد العزل كان القول للوكيل إن كان المبيع مستهلكا، وإن كان قائما فالقول قول الموكل. وكذا في مسألة الغلة لا يصدق في الغلة القائمة. ومما وافق الأصل ما في النهاية : لو أعتق أمة ثم قال لها قطعت يدك وأنت أمتي، فقالت هي قطعتها وأنا حرة فالقول قولها. وكذا في كل شيء أخذه منها عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف رحمه الله ، ذكره قبيل الشهادات .
أقر حربي
6
·
الضمان
وتحتاج هذه المسائل إلى نظر دقيق للفرق بينها وفي المجمع من الإقرار ولو أسلم بأخذ المال قبل الإسلام أو بإتلاف خمر بعده، أو مسلم بمال حربي في دار الحرب أو بقطع يد معتقه قبل العتق ; فكذبوه في الإسناد. أفتى بعدم في الكل. (انتهى) يعني محمد، وقالا يضمن. ومما فرع عليه; لو اشترى عبدا ثم ظهر أنه كان مريضًا ومات عند المشتري ; فإنه لا يرجع بالثمن لأن المرض يتزايد فيحصل الموت بالزائد فلا يضاف إلى السابق، لكن يرجع بنقصان العيب كما ذكره الزيلعي. وليس من فروعها ما إذا تزوج أمة ثم اشتراها ثم ولدت ولدا، يحتمل أن يكون حادثا بعد الشراء أو قبله; فإنه لا شك عندنا في كونها أم ولد لا من جهة أنه حادث أُضيف إلى أقرب أوقاته ; لأنها لو ولدت قبل الشراء ثم ملكها تصير أُم ولده عندنا .
Rule: The principle is to attribute an event to its nearest time. From it is what we have presented: regarding someone who sees impurity on his garment and prayed in it and does not know when it struck him, he repeats the prayer from the last impurity he incurred; and for semen, from the last sleep, he must perform ghusl in the second sleep according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, even if he does not remember a wet dream. In al-Bada'i', he repeats from the last time he had a wet dream, and it is said for urine, consider the last time he urinated, and for blood, the last time he had a nosebleed. If one tears open a cloak and finds a dead mouse and does not know when it entered, if there is no hole, he repeats prayer from the day he placed cotton in it; if there is a hole, he repeats from three days. The two shaykhs acted on this rule and ruled the well impure if a dead mouse is found in it from the time of knowledge without repeating anything, because its occurrence is an event attributed to its nearest time. The Greatest Imam disagreed and considered it better to repeat prayer of three days if it is swollen or rotten, otherwise from a day and a night, acting on the apparent cause and not the imaginary, as a precaution, like a wounded person who did not leave his bed until he died, so it is attributed to the wound. Among its applications: if a man has a slave in his hand, and a man says, 'I gouged his eye' while it was in the seller's possession, and the buyer says, 'I gouged it while in my possession,' the buyer's word is taken and he takes the compensation. Also: if a woman claims her husband divorced her during illness and he became a fugitive, so she inherits; the heirs say he divorced her in health so she does not inherit; her word is taken and she inherits. Excluded from this principle is the issue of al-Kanz from various judicial issues: if a dhimmi dies and his wife says she converted after his death, and the heirs say she converted before his death, the heirs' word is taken, although the mentioned principle would require her word to be taken, and Zufar held this. They excluded from this rule for the sake of strengthening wealth, because the cause of deprivation is established in the present, so it is established in the past. Among what I derived from the principle is in al-Yatimah and others: if someone makes an acknowledgment to an heir and then dies, and the acknowledgee says the acknowledgment was in health, and the heirs say during illness, the heirs' word is taken, and the burden of proof is on the acknowledgee. If he does not provide proof and wants to administer an oath to them, he may do so. (End). Among what is derived from this principle is their statement: if a Muslim dies with a Christian wife, and a Muslim woman comes after his death and says she converted before his death, and the heirs say she converted after his death, the heirs' word is taken, as al-Zayla'i mentioned in various issues. Excluded from this principle: if a judge after removal says to a man, 'I took a thousand from you and paid it to Zaid as a judgment against you,' and the man says, 'You took it unjustly after removal,' the correct view is that the judge's word is taken, although the act is an event that should be attributed to its nearest time, which is the time of removal; some held this and al-Sarakhsi chose it, but the relied-upon is the first because the judge attributed it to a state inconsistent with liability. Similarly, if the one from whom it was taken claims he did it before the appointment of the judge. Also excluded from it is if a slave after emancipation says to another, 'I cut your hand while I was a slave,' and the acknowledgee says, 'You cut it while you were free,' the slave's word is taken. Similarly, if a master says to his slave after emancipating him, 'I took from you five dirhams monthly while you were a slave,' and the freed slave says, 'You took it after emancipation,' the master's word is taken. Similarly, an agent for sale: if he says, 'I sold and delivered before removal' and the principal says 'after removal,' the agent's word is taken if the sold item is consumed; if it remains, the principal's word is taken. Similarly, in the issue of produce, he is not believed regarding produce that remains. What agrees with the principle is in al-Nihayah: if a man emancipates a slave woman and then says, 'I cut your hand while you were my slave,' and she says, 'You cut it while I was free,' her word is taken. Similarly, for everything taken from her according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, mentioned before the chapter on testimonies. Acknowledgment of a Harbi, 6, al-Daman. These issues require precise consideration of the difference between them. In al-Majma' from the chapter on acknowledgment: if a Muslim acknowledges taking wealth before Islam or destroying wine after it, or a Muslim acknowledges wealth of a Harbi in dar al-harb, or acknowledging cutting off the hand of his freed slave before emancipation, and they deny the attribution, Muhammad gave a fatwa of non-liability in all. (End) meaning Muhammad, and the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) said he is liable. Among what is derived from it: if someone buys a slave then it appears he was sick and dies with the buyer, he does not recover the price because illness increases and death occurs from the increase, so it is not attributed to the previous, but he recovers for the defect as al-Zayla'i mentioned. Not from its branches is if someone marries a slave woman, then buys her, then she gives birth, and it is possible the birth occurred after purchase or before; there is no doubt in our school that she becomes umm walad, not because the event is attributed to its nearest time, because if she gave birth before purchase then he owned her, she becomes umm walad in our school.
Source pages p. 55

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi legal maxim 'Events are attributed to the nearest possible time' (الأصل إضافة الحادث إلى أقرب أوقاته). The author begins by stating the maxim and then provides numerous practical applications from various chapters of fiqh, illustrating how the rule operates. The first example involves impurity on a garment discovered after prayer: the prayer is repeated from the last time the person had an impurity event, not from an earlier time. Similar reasoning applies to semen requiring ghusl from the last sleep, urine from the last urination, and blood from the last nosebleed. The text then discusses a case of a dead mouse found in a cloak: if there is no hole, prayer is repeated from the day cotton was placed; if there is a hole (through which the mouse could enter), prayer is repeated from three days. The two shaykhs (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) applied the maxim to a well: if a dead mouse is found, the well water becomes impure from the time of knowledge without requiring re-prayer of past days. However, Imam Abu Hanifa disagreed and ruled based on apparent signs (swelling/rottenness) to determine the time, requiring prayer repetition for three days or one day/night. This shows an internal Hanafi ikhtilaf based on preference for apparent cause over pure attribution. Further applications include disputes over eye-gouging of a slave (buyer's word taken if ownership is contested), and a wife's claim of divorce during illness (her word taken for inheritance). The text then lists exceptions to the maxim: in the case of a dhimmi's wife converting after his death, the heirs' word is taken (contrary to the maxim) to protect financial interests (the wife's inheritance is prevented). Another exception involves a judge after removal: his word is taken regarding payment he made, because his role as judge gives his statement weight. Further exceptions involve slaves after emancipation and agents. The passage concludes with related issues from al-Majma' about acknowledgments of wartime acts and a discussion of a sick slave purchased who dies: the buyer cannot recover the price because death is attributed to the increasing illness, not the pre-existing condition. Finally, the case of a slave woman who gives birth after purchase: she becomes umm walad regardless, not because of the maxim but because ownership after birth establishes the status. The text emphasizes that careful distinction is needed between these cases.

Key Terms
الأرش (al-arsh): Compensation for a defect or injury (e.g., for the gouged eye of a slave).
الفار (al-fār): One who flees; here, the husband who divorced and fled to avoid paying maintenance or inheritance?
الكنز (al-kanz): Treasure; a specific legal issue involving treasure trove. Here, it is an exception to the maxim in judicial disputes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a judge after removal who claims he paid a sum, whose word is taken according to the relied-upon position?
A) The judge's word
B) The defendant's word
C) The word of the person to whom payment was made
D) Neither; only written evidence is accepted

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'attribution to the nearest time' is a default rule but has well-defined exceptions that the mufti must know to issue correct fatwas.

Attributing Events to the Nearest Time: Rule and Exceptions
Explains the qawida and its exceptions with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida of attributing events to the nearest time and identify when istithnā' override it in cases involving judges, slaves, agents, and others.

Core Idea
Events are attributed to the nearest time unless a special legal presumption overrides.

In disputes over when an event occurred, Hanafi jurists generally default to the nearest time. However, this rule is not absolute. This passage from al-Ashbāh wa al-Naẓā'ir presents the maxim and then lists several exceptions where the word is given to a party based on their status or the nature of the claim, such as a judge's presumption of proper conduct or a master's authority over his slave's income.

Guided Path
1
State the qawida
Begin by memorizing the maxim: 'الأصل في الحوادث أن تضاف إلى أقرب أوقاتها'. Understand that this is the default rule in disputes about timing.
This is a wusta (intermediate) rank qawida, applicable in testimony, judgment, and liability.
2
Understand the first case (original rule applied)
Analyze the case of the Muslim woman claiming conversion before her husband's death. The heirs deny, and the word is given to them because the event (conversion) is recent and they are denying a claim that would affect inheritance.
This is an example where the nearest time (after death) is not the claim; the heirs' denial is preferred.
3
Identify the exceptions (istithnā')
List the exceptions: judge after dismissal (word to judge), slave after emancipation (word to slave), master claiming income from freed slave (word to master), agent claiming sale before revocation (word to agent if goods consumed, else principal). For each, note why the default is overridden.
The exceptions share common features: a legal presumption (e.g., judge's propriety, master's authority) overrides the temporal attribution.
4
Distinguish the case confirming the original rule
Contrast the slave girl case: a man frees her, then claims he cut her hand while she was his slave. Her word is taken because she denies the claim, and the event is attributed to the nearest time (after freedom) per the original rule.
Here no overriding presumption exists, so the default applies.
5
Apply the maxim to a new scenario
Given a dispute between a buyer and seller about when goods were delivered, determine who is believed based on the principles from the passage.
Practice: If the seller claims delivery before revocation and the goods are consumed, the seller is believed (like the agent). If goods exist, the buyer is believed (like the principal).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل قولهم ؛ لو مات مسلم وتحته نصرانية فجاءت مسلمة بعد موته، وقالت أسلمت قبل موته وقالت الورثة أسلمت بعد موته ؛ فالقول لهم كما ذكره الزيلعي في مسائل شتى. ومما خرج عن هذا الأصل؛ لو قال القاضي بعد عزله لرجل أخذت منك ألفًا ودفعتها إلى زيد قضيت بها عليك فقال الرجل أخذتها ظلمًا بعد العزل؛ فالصحيح أن القول للقاضي مع أن الفعل حادث، فكان ينبغي أن يضاف إلى أقرب أوقاته وهو وقت العزل، وبه قال البعض واختاره السرخسي. لكن المعتمد الأول لأن القاضي أسنده إلى حالة منافية للضمان. وكذلك إذا زعم المأخوذ منه أنه فعله قبل تقليد القضاء. وخرج أيضًا عنه ما لو قال العبد لغيره بعد العتق قطعت يدك وأنا عبد. وقال المقرّ له بل قطعتها وأنت حرّ، كان القول للعبد وكذا لو قال المولى لعبده، وقد أعتقه، أخذت منك غلة كل شهر خمسة دراهم وأنت عبد ، فقال المعتق أخذتها بعد العتق، كان القول قول المولى. وكذا الوكيل بالبيع؛ إذا قال بعت وسلمت قبل العزل وقال الموكل بعد العزل كان القول للوكيل إن كان المبيع مستهلكا، وإن كان قائما فالقول قول الموكل. وكذا في مسألة الغلة لا يصدق في الغلة القائمة. ومما وافق الأصل ما في النهاية : لو أعتق أمة ثم قال لها قطعت يدك وأنت أمتي، فقالت هي قطعتها وأنا حرَّة فالقول قولها. وكذا في كل شيء أخذه منها عند أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف رحمه الله ، ذكره قبيل الشهادات .
The original rule is their statement: If a Muslim dies and his wife is a Christian, and a Muslim woman comes after his death saying 'I embraced Islam before his death' and the heirs say 'She embraced Islam after his death,' then the word is theirs (the heirs) as mentioned by al-Zayla'i in various issues. Among what deviates from this original rule: if a judge after his dismissal says to a man 'I took a thousand from you and gave it to Zaid as a judgment against you,' and the man says 'You took it unjustly after dismissal,' the correct view is that the word is of the judge, even though the action is recent. It should have been attributed to the nearest time, i.e. the time of dismissal. Some said this and al-Sarakhsi chose it. But the relied-upon is the first because the judge attributed it to a state incompatible with liability. Similarly, if the victim claims that he did it before the judge's appointment. Also deviates from it: if a slave after emancipation says to another 'I cut off your hand while I was a slave,' and the recipient says 'Rather, you cut it while you were free,' the word is of the slave. Also if a master says to his slave whom he has freed 'I took five dirhams from you as monthly income while you were a slave,' and the freedman says 'You took it after emancipation,' the word is of the master. Also the agent for sale: if he says 'I sold and delivered before revocation' and the principal says 'after revocation,' the word is of the agent if the object is consumed; if it still exists, the word is of the principal. Also in the issue of income, he is not believed regarding the existing income. Among what agrees with the original rule is what is in al-Nihaya: if a man frees a slave girl then says to her 'I cut off your hand while you were my slave,' and she says 'You cut it while I was free,' the word is hers. Similarly regarding anything he took from her according to Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on them, mentioned before the chapter on testimonies.
Source pages p. 56

Commentary
This passage presents the qawida (maxim) that in disputes over the timing of an event, the original rule is to attribute the event to the nearest time, but there are many exceptions. The author begins by citing a case from al-Zayla'i: a Muslim dies, his wife is Christian, and a Muslim woman claims she converted before his death; the heirs deny, and the ruling is that the heirs' word is taken because they deny a recent claim. Then the author lists exceptions where the original rule is overturned: a judge after dismissal claims he collected money and paid it as judgment; his word is taken, even though the event is recent, because his claim is incompatible with liability. Similarly, a slave after emancipation claims he injured someone while enslaved; his word is taken. A master after freeing a slave claims he took income while the slave was still enslaved; the master's word is taken. An agent who claims he sold before revocation is believed if the goods are consumed; if they exist, the principal is believed. Finally, a case that agrees with the original rule: a man frees a slave girl and claims he cut her hand while she was his slave; her word is taken because she denies a claim that would affect her freedom. These examples illustrate the complexity of applying the rule of attribution to the nearest time.

Key Terms
الأصل (al-asl): The original rule or default presumption in the absence of contrary evidence.
القول (al-qawl): The statement or claim that is given weight; 'the word is to so-and-so' means they are believed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of the judge after dismissal who claims he collected money and paid it as judgment, whose word is taken?
A) The man who paid the money.
B) The judge.
C) The heirs.
D) The agent.

Key Takeaway
The qawida of attributing events to the nearest time is a default that can be overridden by legal presumptions tied to the status of the claimant.

The Maxim: Original Ruling of Private Parts is Prohibition and Its Applications
Explanation of the maxim and its furuat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida 'al-asl fi al-abda' al-tahrim' to cases of uncertainty in marriage and sexual relations, and identify its exceptions.

Core Idea
When it comes to sexual relations, prohibition is the default until a permissible cause is verified.

This lesson introduces one of the foundational maxims in Hanafi family law: the original ruling regarding private parts (abda') is prohibition. We will explore how this maxim governs cases of uncertainty, such as when a man forgets which slave girl he freed or which wife he divorced. The passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir provides a detailed exposition of this qawida and its numerous furuat (branches), including key exceptions when the cause of prohibition itself is doubtful.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qawida and its source
Locate the maxim in the passage and note that it is quoted from Kashf al-Asrar. Understand that this is a well-established Hanafi rule.
The maxim is: الأصل في الأبضاع التحريم
2
Analyze the furuat from the passage
Work through each case presented: forgetting which slave was freed, forgetting which wife was divorced, sale and return of slaves, etc. Observe how the maxim dictates prohibition and prevents taharri.
In each case, the judge intervenes to resolve uncertainty; the individual cannot act on conjecture.
3
Examine the istithna'at
Identify the two exceptions: (1) when the cause of prohibition itself is doubtful (doubt about milk reaching stomach), and (2) the rare concession to prevent closure of marriage (many potential wet nurses).
In the first exception, the original permissibility remains; in the second, marriage is allowed as a rukhsah.
4
Apply to a new scenario
Use the maxim to derive a ruling for a hypothetical: A man is unsure if his deceased father married a certain woman. Can he marry her? Since the cause of prohibition (father's marriage) is uncertain, the maxim does not apply; default permissibility remains.
This scenario parallels the doubt-about-milk case.
5
Evaluate the fatwa hierarchy
Understand that this maxim is from the later mutakhkhirin (e.g., Ibn Nujaym) but relies on earlier zahir riwayah. The mufta bihi is clear: prohibition when the cause is verified, permissibility when doubtful.
The common error is applying the maxim absolutely without checking verification of the cause.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قاعدة : الأصل في الأبضاع التحريم ولذا قال في كشف الأسرار شرح فخر الإسلام: الأصل في النكاح الحظر، وأبيح للضرورة (انتهى). فإذا تقابل في المرأة حلّ وحرمة غلبت الحرمة، ولهذا لا يجوز التحرِّي في الفروج. وفي كافي الحاكم الشهيد من باب التحري ولو أن رجلاً له أربع جواري أعتق واحدة منهنَّ بعينها، ثم نسيها فلم يدر أيتهنَّ أعتق لم يسعه أن يتحرى للوطء ولا للبيع، ولا يسع الحاكم أن يخلي بينه وبينهنَّ حتى يبين المعتقة من غيرها، وكذلك إذا طلق إحدى نسائه بعينها ثلاثا ثم نسيها، وكذلك إن ميَّز كلهنَّ إلا واحدة؛ لم يسعه أن يقربها حتى يعلم أنها غير المطلقة وكذلك يمنعه القاضي عنها حتى يخبر أنها غير المطلقة، فإذا أخبر بذلك استحلفه ألبتة أنه ما طلق هذه بعينها ثلاثا ثم خلى بينهما ؛ فإن كان حلف وهو جاهل بها فلا ينبغي له أن يقربها فإن باع في المسألة الأولى ثلاثا من الجواري فحكم الحاكم؛ فإن أجاز بيعهنَّ وكان ذلك من رأيه وجعل الباقية المعتقة، ثم رجع إليه بعض ما باع بشراء أو هبة أو ميراث لم ينبغ له أن يطأها (هي) لأن القاضي قضى فيه بغير علم فلا ينبغي له أن يطأ شيئًا منهنَّ بالملك إلا أن يتزوجها فحينئذ لا بأس لأنها زوجته أو أمته ولا يجوز التحرّي في الفروج لأنه يجوز في كل ما جار للضرورة والفروج لا تحلّ بالضرورة (انتهى). ثم قال؛ ولو أعتق جارية من رقيقه ثم نسيها ومات لم يجز للقاضي التحرّي، ولا يقول للورثة أعتقوا أيتهنَّ شئتم أو أعتقوا التي أكبر ظنكم أنها حرَّة ولكنه يسألهم فإن زعموا أن الميت أعتق هذه بعينها ،أعتقها واستحلفهم على علمهم في الباقيات؛ فإن لم يعرفوا من ذلك شيئًا أعتقهنَّ كلهنَّ وأسقط عنهنَّ قيمة إحداهن وسعين فيما بقي. (انتهى). وخرج عن هذا الأصل مسألة في فتاوى قاضي خان؛ صبية أرضعها قوم كثير من أهل القرية ؛ أقلهم أو أكثرهم لا يدرى من أرضعها وأراد واحد من أهل القرية أن يتزوجها. قال أبو القاسم الصفار : إذا لم تظهر له علامة ولا يشهد أحد له بذلك يجوز نكاحها. وهذا من باب الرخصة كيلا ينسدّ باب النكاح. فلو اختلطت الرضيعة بنساء يحصون لم أره الآن ثم رأيت في الكافي للحاكم الشهيد ما يفيد الحل، ولفظه ولو أن قوما كان لكل منهم جارية فأعتق أحدهم جاريته ولم يعرفوا المعتقة (۱) فلكل واحد منهم أن يطأ جاريته حتى يعلم أنها المعتقة بعينها، وإن كان أكبر رأي أحدهم أنه هو الذي أعتق فأحبّ إليَّ أنه لا يقرب حتى يستيقن ذلك، ولو قرب لم يكن ذلك حراما، ولو اشتراهن رجل واحد قد علم ذلك لم يحلّ له أن يقرب واحدة منهنَّ حتى يعرف المعتقة، ولو اشتراهنَّ إلا واحدة حلَّ له وطؤهنَّ، فإن فعل ثم اشترى الباقية لم يحلّ له وطء شيء منهنَّ ولا بيعه حتى يعلم المعتقة منهن. (انتهى). ثم اعلم أن هذه القاعدة إنما هي فيما إذا كان في المرأة سبب محقق للحرمة، فلو كان في الحرمة شك لم يعتبر ؛ ولذا قالوا لو أدخلت المرأة حلمة ثديها في فم رضيعة ووقع الشك في وصول اللبن إلى جوفها لم تحرم لأن في المانع شكا، كما في الولوالجية. وفي القنية : امرأة كانت تعطي ثديها صبية واشتهر ذلك فيما بينهم، ثم تقول لم يكن في ثديي لبن حين ألقمتها ثديي ولم يعلم ذلك إلا من جهتها؛ جاز لابنها أن يتزوج بهذه الصبية .(انتهى). وفي الخانية صغير وصغيرة بينهما شبهة الرضاع ولا يعلم ذلك حقيقة ؛ قالوا لا بأس بالنكاح بينهما هذا إذا لم يخبر بذلك
Rule: The original ruling of private parts is prohibition. Hence it is said in Kashf al-Asrar, commentary of Fakhral Islam: The original ruling of marriage is prohibition, and it was permitted out of necessity. So when permissibility and prohibition conflict in a woman, prohibition prevails, and therefore taharri is not allowed in matters of private parts. In Kafi al-Hakim al-Shahid, from the chapter on taharri: If a man has four slave girls and frees one specific one, then forgets which one he freed, he cannot engage in taharri for intercourse or sale, nor may the judge allow him to be with them until the freed one is distinguished from the others. Similarly, if he divorces one of his wives thrice and forgets which one, and if he segregates all but one, he may not approach her until he knows she is not the divorced one, and the judge prevents him until he reports she is not divorced. If he reports that, the judge administers an oath that he has not divorced this specific one thrice, then leaves them together. If he swore while ignorant, he should not approach her. If in the first issue he sold three of the slave girls and the judge ratified the sale and considered the remaining one as freed, then some of what was sold returned to him via purchase, gift, or inheritance, he should not have intercourse with her [any of them], because the judge ruled without knowledge, so he should not have intercourse with any of them by virtue of ownership unless he marries her, in which case it is fine because she is his wife or his slave girl. Taharri is not permitted in private parts because it is permitted in everything that is necessary, but private parts are not permitted out of necessity. Then he said: If he frees a slave girl and forgets which one and dies, the judge may not engage in taharri, nor tell the heirs to free whichever they wish or free the one they most strongly believe to be free. Rather, he asks them: if they claim the deceased freed this specific one, he frees her and administers an oath on their knowledge regarding the remaining ones. If they do not know anything of that, he frees them all and drops from them the value of one of them, and they work to earn what remains. Excepted from this rule is a case in Fatawa Qadi Khan: a young girl who was breastfed by many people from a village, fewer or more, and it is unknown who breastfed her. One of the villagers wants to marry her. Abu al-Qasim al-Saffar said: If no sign appears and no one testifies, it is permissible to marry her. This is a concession to prevent the closure of marriage. If the breastfed girl mingles with women who are countable, I have not seen it now. Then I saw in al-Kafi by al-Hakim al-Shahid something indicating permissibility: its wording: If a group of people each have a slave girl, and one of them freed his slave girl, and they do not know who is freed, then each can have intercourse with his slave girl until he knows she is the one freed. If the strongest opinion of one of them is that he is the one who freed, I prefer that he does not approach until he is certain, but if he approaches it is not forbidden. If one man bought all of them knowing this, he may not approach any until he knows the freed one. If he bought all but one, he may have intercourse with them; if after that he buys the remaining, he may not have intercourse with any of them nor sell them until he knows which is freed. Then know that this rule applies only when there is a verified cause of prohibition in the woman. If the prohibition is doubtful, it is not considered. Hence they said: If a woman puts her nipple in the mouth of a nursling and there is doubt whether milk reached her stomach, she does not become prohibited because there is doubt in the preventing factor, as in al-Walwalijiyya. In al-Qunyah: A woman used to give her breast to a girl, and this was well-known among them. Then she says there was no milk in my breast when I put it in her mouth, and this is only known from her. It is permissible for her son to marry this girl. In al-Khaniyyah: A boy and a girl between whom there is suspicion of breastfeeding but the reality is not known, they said: No problem in marriage between them, this if he does not inform about it.
Source pages p. 57

Commentary
This passage presents the maxim 'The original ruling of private parts (abda') is prohibition' and applies it to various cases of uncertainty regarding marriage and sexual relations. The author begins by quoting Kashf al-Asrar: the original ruling of marriage is prohibition, permitted only out of necessity (i.e., for procreation and companionship). Whenever permissibility and prohibition conflict regarding a woman, prohibition prevails. Consequently, taharri (effort to determine the correct ruling) is not allowed in matters of private parts (furuuj), because they are not permitted out of necessity. The author then presents a series of illustrative cases from Kafi al-Hakim: a man who frees one slave girl and forgets which one; he cannot use taharri to decide which to have intercourse with or sell; the judge must step in to resolve the ambiguity. Similarly for divorce. If the man sells three of the four and the judge validates the sale, the remaining one is presumed freed; if the sold ones later return to him via various means, he still cannot have intercourse with any by ownership, only by marriage. Further, if the man dies after forgetting which slave he freed, the judge does not apply taharri but investigates with the heirs. Exceptions to this strict rule are mentioned: a girl breastfed by many villagers where the specific foster-mother is unknown; marriage is allowed as a concession to prevent the closure of marriage. Also from al-Kafi: in a group where each man has a slave girl and one frees his, each may continue intercourse until the freed one is identified; but if one man buys all, he cannot until knowledge. The passage then qualifies the maxim: the rule applies only when the cause of prohibition is verified (muhaqqaq); if there is doubt about the cause itself (e.g., doubt whether milk reached the stomach in breastfeeding), the prohibition does not apply, and the original permissibility (ibaha) remains. Examples from al-Walwalijiyya, al-Qunyah, and al-Khaniyyah illustrate this qualification.

Key Terms
الأبضاع (al-abda'): Private parts; used juristically to mean the object of sexual intercourse and the permissibility thereof.
التحرّي (al-taharrī): Making an effort to determine the likely permissible action when the actual ruling is unknown; not allowed in matters of private parts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a man prohibited from having intercourse with any of his slave girls if he has forgotten which one he freed?
A) Because all slave girls become automatically prohibited due to the freedom of one
B) Because the original ruling of private parts is prohibition, and the identity of the freed one is uncertain, so prohibition prevails
C) Because the judge has not given permission
D) Because he must perform an expiation (kaffarah)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi default in sexual matters is prohibition; the burden of proof is on permissibility.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate
12 min
Literal Interpretation in Oaths and Legal Statements
Collection of rulings applying the maxim of literal meaning in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-ayman tubna 'ala al-haqiqah' to determine the scope of an oath, bequest, or acknowledgment, and identify when exceptions based on custom or necessity apply.

Core Idea
Oaths, bequests, and acknowledgments are taken literally: 'child' means direct child, 'sell' means direct action—unless inability or custom requires otherwise.

This lesson explores a foundational principle in Hanafi fiqh: that legal statements (oaths, bequests, acknowledgments) are interpreted according to the literal meaning of the words used. Through a rich collection of examples from classical texts, you will learn how to apply this maxim consistently, and recognize when exceptions arise due to custom, necessity, or the nature of the speaker.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal statement
Determine whether the statement is an oath, bequest, acknowledgment, or similar. Each type has its own nuances, but the literal meaning principle applies broadly.
e.g., 'I swear not to sell', 'I bequeath to my children', 'This house is Zaid's'.
2
Determine the literal meaning of the key term
Look at the word in its linguistic and customary context. What is the primary, original meaning? For example, 'sell' means direct sale, 'child' means direct offspring, 'eat from this sheep' means meat.
Use trusted Arabic lexicons and Hanafi usul works to ascertain haqiqah.
3
Apply the literal meaning to the scenario
Does the actual action or object match the literal meaning exactly? If yes, the ruling applies; if not, the oath is not broken or the bequest does not include the extension.
Example: Swearing not to eat from a sheep – milk and offspring are not literal eating, so no breach.
4
Check for exceptions
Consider if any exception overrides the literal meaning: the speaker cannot perform directly (e.g., judge), strong custom ('urf), or necessity (e.g., protection of life).
If the swearer is a judge, agency breaks the oath because direct action is impossible.
5
Conclude the ruling
Based on the above, state the final legal ruling: does the action break the oath? Who inherits? Is the acknowledgment valid?
Always tie back to the maxim and the specific evidence from the source.
