Seven Types of Legal Alleviations in Sharia
Classifying the seven categories of rukhsah in Islamic law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the seven types of legal alleviations (takhfifat) in Islamic law and apply each type to concrete examples.

Core Idea
Sharia alleviations are not just removal; they include reduction, substitution, advancement, delay, permission, and change of form.

Islamic law is built on principles that accommodate human hardship. This lesson introduces the seven categories of legal alleviations (takhfifat) as classified in the Hanafi tradition. Understanding these categories helps a mufti identify the appropriate ruling when a concession is needed.

Guided Path
1
Memorize the seven categories
Learn the Arabic names: isqat, tanqis, ibdal, taqdim, ta'khir, tarkhis, taghyir. Associate each with a key example.
Use the mnemonic: Remove, Reduce, Substitute, Advance, Delay, Permit, Change.
2
Understand the definition of each type
For each category, identify whether it removes, reduces, substitutes, advances, delays, permits, or changes the original ruling.
Note that 'tarkhis' permits what is normally prohibited, not merely reducing an obligation.
3
Analyze the examples given
Go through each example in the text: tayammum, qasr, combining prayers, etc. Understand how each fits its category.
For tanqis, note the intra-Hanafi difference on whether qasr is original or a reduction.
4
Test with new scenarios
For a given hardship (e.g., illness, travel, fear), determine which type(s) of alleviation apply.
A single situation may involve multiple types, e.g., prayer of fear involves taghyir and tarkhis.
5
Apply in fatwa context
When issuing a fatwa, identify the relevant takhfif type and cite the basis for the concession.
The classification helps ensure the fatwa is grounded in usul, not mere leniency.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفائدة الثانية :
تخفيفات الشرع أنواع :
الأول : تخفيف إسقاط كإسقاط العبادات عند وجود أعذارها .
الثاني : تخفيف تنقيص : كالقصر في السفر على القول بأن الإتمام أصل. وأما على قول من قال القصر أصل والإتمام فرض بعده، فلا إلا صورة.
الثالث: تخفيف إبدال كإبدال الوضوء والغسل بالتيمم، والقيام في الصلاة
بالقعود والاضطجاع والركوع والسجود بالإيماء والصيام بالإطعام.
الرابع تخفيف تقديم كالجمع بعرفات وتقديم الزكاة على الحول وزكاة الفطر في رمضان وقبله على الصحيح بعد تملك النصاب في الأول ووجود الرأس بصفة المؤنة والولاية في الثاني.
الخامس : تخفيف تأخير كالجمع بمزدلفة، وتأخير رمضان للمريض والمسافر، وتأخير الصلاة عن وقتها في حق مشتغل بإنقاذ غريق ونحوه .
السادس : تخفيف ترخيص كصلاة المستجمر مع بقية النجو، وشرب الخمر
للغصة .
السابع : تخفيف تغيير ؛ كتغيير نظم الصلاة للخوف.
The second benefit: The alleviations of the Shariah are types: First: alleviation of removal, like removal of acts of worship when their excuses exist. Second: alleviation of reduction, like shortening prayer in travel on the view that completion is the original. But on the view that shortening is original and completion is subsequent obligation, then it is not, except in one form. Third: alleviation of substitution, like substituting ablution and ghusl with tayammum, and standing in prayer with sitting, lying down, bowing and prostrating with gestures, and fasting with feeding. Fourth: alleviation of advancement, like combining at Arafat, and advancing zakat before the year, and zakat al-fitr in Ramadan and before it according to the correct view, after owning the nisab in the first, and the existence of the head with the condition of provision and guardianship in the second. Fifth: alleviation of delay, like combining at Muzdalifa, delaying Ramadan for the sick and traveler, and delaying prayer from its time for one occupied with rescuing a drowning person and the like. Sixth: alleviation of permission, like the prayer of one who uses stones with traces of impurity remaining, and drinking wine for choking. Seventh: alleviation of change, like changing the arrangement of prayer due to fear.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage from Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir provides a systematic classification of the types of legal alleviations (takhfifat) in Islamic law. The author enumerates seven categories: (1) removal of an obligation entirely when an excuse exists, (2) reduction in the quantity, such as shortening prayer in travel (noting the difference of opinion on whether full prayer is the original or short prayer is the original), (3) substitution of one act for another, e.g., tayammum for wudu/ghusl, sitting for standing in prayer, gestures for bowing and prostrating, and feeding for fasting, (4) advancement of an obligation before its usual time, like combining at Arafat and paying zakat before the year, (5) delay beyond the usual time, such as combining at Muzdalifa, delaying Ramadan for the sick and traveler, and delaying prayer for rescuing a drowning person, (6) permission to do what is normally prohibited, such as praying with traces of impurity after istijmar and drinking wine to save from choking, and (7) change in the form of worship, like altering the order of prayer due to fear. Each type illustrates how the Shariah accommodates necessity and hardship.

Key Terms
تخفيف إسقاط (takhfif isqat): Alleviation by complete removal of an obligation when an excuse exists.
تخفيف تنقيص (takhfif tanqis): Alleviation by reducing the quantity or extent of an obligation.
تخفيف إبدال (takhfif ibdal): Alleviation by substituting one obligation with another easier act.
تخفيف تقديم (takhfif taqdim): Alleviation by performing an obligation before its usual time.
تخفيف تأخير (takhfif ta'khir): Alleviation by delaying an obligation beyond its usual time.
تخفيف ترخيص (takhfif tarkhis): Alleviation by permitting what is normally prohibited due to necessity.
تخفيف تغيير (takhfif taghyir): Alleviation by changing the form or arrangement of an act of worship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) تخفيف إسقاط
B) تخفيف إبدال
C) تخفيف تنقيص
D) تخفيف ترخيص

Key Takeaway
The seven types of takhfifat form a comprehensive framework for understanding how Sharia accommodates hardship while preserving the spirit of the law.

The Rule of Hardship and Text: Al-Mashaqqah wa al-Nass
Parsing a legal maxim with two illustrative furu'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim that hardship is not considered in the presence of a text, apply it to furu' cases, and evaluate the intra-madhhab dispute between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf.

Core Idea
Hardship never overrides a clear text—the nass is the final arbiter.

This lesson unpacks a key Hanafi maxim that restrains the use of hardship as a grounds for leniency: the text (nass) takes precedence. We will see how Abu Hanifa and Muhammad held firm to textual rulings despite claims of widespread hardship, while Abu Yusuf considered the burden on the community. Two illustrative cases—grazing in the Haram and the impurity of animal droppings—show the maxim in action and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
First, memorize the Arabic maxim: 'المشقة والحرج إنما يعتبران في موضع لا نص فيه'. Understand that it is a restrictive sub-maxim (dabt) governing fatwa: hardship (mashaqqah) and difficulty (haraj) are only considered when there is no explicit text (nass). If a text exists, it overrides any claim of hardship.
This is a sughra (minor) maxim that restricts the application of the broader principle of taysir (facilitation).
2
Analyze the first furu': grazing in the Haram
Apply the maxim to the case: The text forbids cutting or grazing the grass of the Haram (except idhkhir). Abu Hanifa and Muhammad upheld this prohibition despite the hardship it imposes. Abu Yusuf argued for permission due to general hardship, but he was rebutted because the text is clear.
The dispute shows that even within the Hanafi school, some may consider hardship, but the majority position rejects it when nass exists.
3
Analyze the second furu': impurity of animal droppings
The hadith declares animal droppings as 'riks' (impure). Abu Hanifa considered them heavily impure (najis ghalizah) despite their widespread presence (balwa). He argued that the text overrides the general affliction. Abu Yusuf again considered the hardship of avoidance, but the textual evidence prevailed.
Compare with human urine: even more widespread, yet still impure due to the text. This reinforces the maxim.
4
Evaluate the intra-madhhab dispute
Identify the two positions: (1) Abu Hanifa and Muhammad: text overrides hardship. (2) Abu Yusuf: hardship can override if widespread. Then note the tarjih: the relied-upon view (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, as it is based on explicit texts and supported by the majority of later Hanafi scholars.
The maxim 'ma 'ammat baliyatuhu khaffat qadiyatuhu' (what is widespread is light) is acknowledged but only applies when no nass contradicts it.
5
Synthesize: how a mufti applies this maxim
When issuing a fatwa, the mufti must first check if there is a clear text from Quran or authentic hadith on the matter. If yes, that ruling stands, even if it entails hardship. Only in the absence of a text may hardship be considered for concessions.
This maxim prevents arbitrary leniency and preserves the binding force of revealed rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفائدة الثالثة :
·
المشقة والحرج؛ إنما يعتبران في موضع لا نص فيه، وأما مع النص بخلافه فلا، ولذا قال أبو حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله بحرمة رعي حشيش الحرم وقطعه إلا الإذخر. وجوّز أبو يوسف رحمه الله رعيه للحرج وردَّ عليه بما ذكرناه، وذكره الزيلعي في جنايات الإحرام وقال في الأنجاس إن الإمام يقول بتغليظ نجاسة الأرواث، لقوله عليه السلام إنها ركس أي نجس ولا اعتبار عنده بالبلوى في موضع النص، كما في بول الآدمي فإن البلوى فيه أعم. (انتهى). وفي شرح منية المصلي: من المتأخرين من زاد في تفسير الغليظة على قول أبي حنيفة رحمه الله. ولا حرج في اجتنابه كما في الاختيار، وفي الغليظة على قولهما ولا بلوى في إصابته كما في الاختيار أيضًا. وفي المحيط وهي زيادة حسنة يشهد لها بعض فروع الباب. والمراد بقوله ولا حرج في اجتنابه ولا بلوى في إصابته على اختلاف العبارتين، إنما هو بالنسبة إلى جنس المكلفين فيقع الاتفاق على صدق القضية المشهورة وهي : إن ما عمت بليته خفت قضيته . (انتهى).
The third benefit: Hardship and difficulty are only considered in a place where there is no text. But when there is a text to the contrary, they are not. Hence Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them) said the prohibition of grazing the grass of the Haram and cutting it except idhkhir. Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) permitted grazing it due to hardship, and he was rebutted with what we mentioned. Al-Zayla'i mentioned it in the chapter on ihram offenses and said concerning impurities that the Imam (Abu Hanifa) holds the intensification of impurity of animal droppings because of his saying (peace be upon him) that they are riks (impure), and he does not consider general affliction in the presence of a text, as in human urine, for the affliction is more general. (End). And in Sharh Munyat al-Musalli: Some later scholars added in the interpretation of 'heavy' (ghalizah) according to Abu Hanifa's saying. There is no hardship in avoiding it, as in al-Ikhtiyar, and regarding 'heavy' according to the two, there is no affliction in hitting it, as also in al-Ikhtiyar. And in al-Muhit, it is a good addition attested by some branches of the chapter. And the intended meaning of his statement 'There is no hardship in avoiding it' and 'There is no affliction in hitting it' according to the different expressions is only in relation to the generality of the legally responsible, so agreement occurs on the truth of the famous proposition: 'What is widespread in affliction is light in its ruling.' (End).
Source pages p. 72

Commentary
This passage discusses a fundamental rule of Hanafi usul al-iftā: hardship (mashaqqah) and difficulty (haraj) are only considered in the absence of a text (nass). When a clear textual ruling exists, it cannot be overridden by claims of hardship. The author illustrates with two examples: First, the dispute regarding grazing grass in the Haram. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad prohibited it because of the general prohibition, while Abu Yusuf permitted it due to hardship. The rebuttal is that the text prohibits, so hardship does not justify violation. Second, the issue of impurity: Abu Hanifa holds animal droppings as heavily impure based on hadith, despite their widespread presence, because the text overrides general affliction (balwa). The Sharh Munyat al-Musalli explains that 'no hardship in avoiding it' applies to the general public, not individuals, agreeing with the maxim 'what is widespread in affliction is light in its ruling.'

Key Terms
المشقة (al-mashaqqah): Hardship or difficulty that typically accompanies an obligation; in this context, it is a factor that may lighten a ruling only in the absence of a nass
الحرج (al-haraj): Constriction or difficulty; similar to mashaqqah but often referring to a situation that would cause undue burden
النص (al-nass): A clear textual ruling from Quran or Sunnah

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on grazing grass in the Haram according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad?
A) Permitted due to hardship
B) Prohibited except idhkhir
C) Permitted only for grazing animals
D) Prohibited completely without any exception

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'hardship is not considered in the presence of a text' ensures that clear revealed rulings are not undermined by subjective claims of difficulty.

The Third Maxim: What Is Permitted by Necessity Is Limited to Its Extent
Stating the third maxim and providing many furuat from different chapters

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim, identify its scope, and apply it to evaluate the permissibility of actions in necessity-based scenarios.

Core Idea
Necessity permits only the minimum; excess is not excused.

This lesson introduces a key restrictive maxim that safeguards against abuse of necessity-based dispensations. While the companion maxim 'الضرورات تبيح المحظورات' opens the door, this maxim limits how much can enter. We will explore its application in diverse fiqh chapters, from ritual purity to medical treatment and warfare.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its rank
Identify the Arabic text of the maxim and note that it is a wusta (intermediate) maxim that restricts the scope of the broader maxim 'الضرورات تبيح المحظورات'.
This maxim is classified as dabt in the Hanafi qawida hierarchy.
2
Identify the companion maxim it restricts
Recall that the maxim 'الضرورات تبيح المحظورات' permits forbidden acts under necessity. The current maxim imposes a quantitative limit: only what is strictly necessary is allowed.
Both maxims together form a balanced approach: necessity opens the door, this maxim prevents overuse.
3
Walk through the furuat
For each branch (carrion, enemy provisions, cat urine, camel dung, used water, martyr's blood, splint, doctor's gaze), identify the necessity and the extent permitted. Note that in some cases (cat urine vessels, others' clothes) the lack of necessity leads to no dispensation.
Pay attention to the relied-upon view: no distinction between desert and city wells for camel dung.
4
Analyze the exception: false oath vs. ta'rid
Note that a false oath is not permitted even under necessity because ta'rid (allusion) suffices to fulfill the need without falsehood. This shows that if a less harmful alternative exists, the dispensation is blocked.
The governing maxim here is 'الأمور بمقاصدها' because intention differentiates between falsehood and allowable ambiguity.
5
Apply the maxim to a new scenario
Take a modern example: a diabetic patient who cannot fast safely during Ramadan may break the fast, but only enough to prevent harm. The maxim limits the breaking to the minimum food/drink required.
The mufti must assess genuine need and the minimum remedy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية : ما أُبيح للضرورة يقدر بقدرها، ولذا قال في أيمان الظهيرية : إن اليمين الكاذبة لا تُباح للضرورة وإنما تُباح التعريض. (انتهى). يعني لاندفاعها بالتعريض. ومن فروعه المضطر لا يأكل من الميتة إلا قدر سد الرمق والطعام في دار الحرب يؤخذ على سبيل الحاجة لأنه إنما أُبيح للضرورة. قال في الكنز : وينتفع فيها بعلف وطعام وحطب وسلاح ودهن بلا قسمة وبعد الخروج منها ،لا، وما فضل ردّ إلى الغنيمة . وأفتوا بالعفو عن بول السنور في الثياب دون الأواني، لأنه لا ضرورة في الأواني لجريان العادة بتخميرها. وفرَّق كثير من المشايخ في البعر بين آبار الفلوات؛ فيعفى عن قليله للضرورة لأنه ليس لها رؤوس حاجزة والإبل تبعر ،حولها، وبين آبار الأمصار لعدم الضرورة بخلاف الكثير ولكن المعتمد عدم الفرق بين آبار الفلوات والأمصار، وبين الصحيح والمنكسر وبين الرطب واليابس. ويعفى عن ثياب المتوضيء إذا الماء المستعمل، وعلى رواية النجاسة للضرورة ولا يعفى عما يصيب ثوب غيره لعدمها، ودم الشهيد طاهر في حق نفسه، نجس في حق غيره لعدم الضرورة، والجبيرة يجب ألا تستر من الصحيح إلا بقدر ما لا بد منه، والطبيب إنما ينظر من العورة بقدر الحاجة. وفرع الشافعية عليها أن المجنون لا يجوز تزويجه أكثر من واحدة لاندفاع الحاجة بها. (انتهى). ولم أره لمشايخنا رحمهم الله.
The second: What is permitted due to necessity is limited to its extent. Hence it is said in Ayman al-Dhahiriyyah: A false oath is not permitted due to necessity; only allusion (ta'rid) is permitted. (End). That is, because the necessity is removed by allusion. Among its branches: the starving person may not eat from carrion except the amount that sustains life (sadd al-ramaq). Food in enemy territory is taken according to need, because it was only permitted for necessity. Al-Kanz says: In the enemy territory, one may benefit from fodder, food, firewood, weapons, and oil without division before leaving; after leaving, no, and what remains is returned to the booty. And they gave fatwa that cat urine in clothes is excused but not in vessels, because there is no necessity in vessels due to the custom of covering them. And many scholars distinguished in the issue of dung (ba'r) between wells in open desert (falawat) — its small amount is excused due to necessity because they have no protective tops and camels dung around them — and wells in cities (amsar) due to lack of necessity, except for large amounts; but the relied-upon view is no distinction between desert wells and city wells, nor between dry and broken, nor between wet and dry. And the clothes of one performing wudu are excused if touched by used water, according to the narration of impurity due to necessity; but what hits the clothes of another is not excused due to lack of necessity. The blood of a martyr is pure for himself, impure for others due to lack of necessity. The splint must not cover more of the sound limb than necessary. The physician may only look at the private parts to the extent of need. The Shafi'is branched upon this that a madman may not be married to more than one wife because the need is fulfilled by one. (End). I have not seen this from our scholars (may Allah have mercy on them).
Source pages p. 73

Commentary
This passage presents the third maxim: 'ما أبيح للضرورة يقدر بقدرها' (What is permitted due to necessity is limited to its extent). The text begins by applying it to false oaths: a false oath (al-yamin al-kadhiba) is not permitted even under necessity; instead, one may use allusion (ta'rid) such as ambiguous wording, because that suffices to remove the necessity without resorting to outright lying. Then numerous branches are listed: (1) The starving person may only eat carrion enough to sustain life (sadd al-ramaq). (2) In enemy territory (dar al-harb), one may take provisions (fodder, food, firewood, weapons, oil) only according to need, and before leaving; after leaving it is not allowed, and leftovers are returned to the general booty — this is from Al-Kanz. (3) Cat urine on clothes is excused (i.e., it does not prevent prayer) due to common occurrence and difficulty of avoidance, but in vessels it is not excused because vessels can be covered easily. (4) Many scholars distinguished between camel dung in desert wells (where it is excused in small amounts due to necessity, because wells lack protective tops and camels dung around them) and city wells (where no necessity exists, so it is not excused except perhaps in large amounts). However, the relied-upon position (al-mu'tamad) is that there is no distinction between desert and city wells, nor between dry and broken, nor between wet and dry splints? Actually the text mentions 'بين الصحيح والمنكسر وبين الرطب واليابس' which refers to the issue of splints? The context is about dung, so the distinction might be about the condition of the dung. The relied view negates any distinction. (5) The clothes of a person performing wudu are excused if touched by used water (considered impure on one narration) due to necessity, but if the water splashes on another's clothes, it is not excused because no necessity. (6) The blood of a martyr is pure for himself (on his body) but impure for others because there is no necessity for others. (7) The splint (jabira) must not cover more of the healthy limb than absolutely necessary. (8) The physician may only look at the private parts to the extent required for treatment. Finally, the Shafi'is derive that a madman may only be married to one wife because one wife suffices for the necessity of preservation of lineage; the author notes he has not seen this from Hanafi scholars.

Key Terms
سد الرمق (sadd al-ramaq): The minimum amount of food to keep one alive
التعريض (al-ta'rid): Using ambiguous wording that hints but does not explicitly state a falsehood

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position, if a person's clothes are touched by used water (considered impure) while performing wudu, what is the ruling?
A) It is excused for the person performing wudu due to necessity
B) It is never excused
C) It is excused only if the water is less than a certain amount
D) It is excused for everyone including others

Key Takeaway
Necessity-based permissions are strictly limited to the minimum required; any excess nullifies the dispensation.

The Principle of Temporal Validity: ما جاز لعذر بطل بزواله
An appendix (tadhnib) presenting a corollary maxim and its furuat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi dabt 'ما جاز لعذر بطل بزواله' to identify when a temporary dispensation (rukhsah) becomes invalid upon the removal of its cause, specifically in cases of tayammum and secondary witness testimony.

Core Idea
A dispensation granted due to an excuse expires the moment the excuse ends.

In Hanafi fiqh, rulings that are granted due to necessity or excuse are inherently temporary. The maxim 'ما جاز لعذر بطل بزواله' (What is allowed due to an excuse becomes invalid upon its removal) captures this temporal dimension. This dabt (sub-maxim) is a corollary of the broader qawida 'الضرورات تبيح المحظورات'. In this lesson, we analyse its application to tayammum (dry ablution) and shahadah ala al-shahadah (secondary witness testimony) as presented in Ibn Nujaym's Al-Ashbah wa al-Nazair.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Begin by reading the maxim: ما جاز لعذر بطل بزواله. Identify that it governs cases where a ruling is temporarily permitted due to an excuse (udhr). The scope includes ibadat (like tayammum) and muamalat (like testimony).
This is a dabt, not a kubra maxim; it is derived from the principle that necessity permits prohibitions.
2
Analyse the tayammum applications
List the three excuses for tayammum: lack of water, illness, cold. For each, identify the event that invalidates the tayammum: ability to use water, recovery, disappearance of cold. Note that invalidation is automatic – no intentional nullification is needed.
The tayammum becomes invalid even if one is in the middle of prayer; the prayer must be broken and performed with wudu.
3
Understand the rationale behind automatic invalidation
The dispensation (tayammum) is a substitute that only remains valid while the excuse exists. Once the obstacle is removed, the original obligation (wudu/ghusl with water) returns immediately. This prevents the dispensation from exceeding its purpose.
Contrast with intentional nullification: invalidation here is external and independent of the person's intent.
4
Extend to secondary testimony (shahadah ala al-shahadah)
Secondary testimony is normally accepted only when the original witness is unable to testify (due to death, illness, or travel). If the original witness recovers from illness or returns from travel, the secondary testimony becomes invalid – because the excuse for using it has disappeared.
The author says 'وينبغي أن تخرج على هذه القاعدة' indicating this is a logical extension of the maxim.
5
Note the internal Hanafi dispute on secondary testimony
The ruling that the secondary testimony becomes invalid is based on the view that it is only permitted for the mentioned excuses. There is another Hanafi view that allows secondary testimony more broadly; under that view, the rule might not apply in the same way.
The passage itself does not resolve the dispute but applies the maxim to one position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تذنيب : يقرت من هذه القاعدة : ما جاز لعذر بطل بزواله فبطل التيمم إذا قدر على استعمال الماء. فإن كان لفَقْد الماء بطل بالقدرة عليه وإن كان لمرض بطل ببرئه، وإن كان لبرد بطل بزواله. وينبغي أن تخرج على هذه القاعدة الشهادة على الشهادة؛ إذا كان الأصل مريضًا فصح بعد الإشهاد أو مسافرًا فقدم أن يبطل الإشهاد على القول بأنها لا تجوز إلا لموت الأصل أو مرضه أو سفره .
Appendix: Close to this rule is: What is allowed due to an excuse becomes invalid upon the removal of that excuse. Thus tayammum becomes invalid when one is able to use water. If it was due to lack of water, it becomes invalid by having the ability to use it; if due to illness, it becomes invalid by recovery; if due to cold, it becomes invalid by its departure. And one should derive from this rule the issue of testimony upon testimony (shahadah ala al-shahadah); if the original witness was ill and then recovered after the testimony, or was a traveller and then returned, then the testimony (the second testimony) becomes invalid according to the view that it is only permitted due to the death, illness, or travel of the original witness.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This appendix (tadhneeb) presents a rule close to the previous one: 'ما جاز لعذر بطل بزواله' (What is allowed due to an excuse becomes invalid upon removal of that excuse). This is not a separate maxim but a corollary of the previous qawida. The example given is tayammum (dry ablution): it is valid as a substitute for wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable due to lack of water, illness, or intense cold. Once the excuse ends—water becomes available, the person recovers from illness, or the cold subsides—the tayammum becomes invalid, meaning one must perform ablution with water before prayer. The text then applies this to the ruling of 'shahadah ala al-shahadah' (testimony based on another witness's testimony). In Hanafi fiqh, secondary witness testimony (i.e., one witness testifying on the basis of another witness's testimony) is normally not accepted except when the original witness is dead, ill, or absent (on a journey). If the original witness was ill or a traveler at the time of giving secondary testimony, but later recovers or returns, then according to one school of thought (that it is only allowed due to those excuses), the secondary testimony becomes invalid because the excuse has ended. This demonstrates the practical application of the rule.

Key Terms
التيمم (al-tayammum): Dry ablution using earth as a substitute for wudu or ghusl when water is unavailable or harmful
الشهادة على الشهادة (al-shahadah ala al-shahadah): Testimony given by a secondary witness based on the testimony of the original witness

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, under which condition does tayammum due to cold become invalid?
A) When the cold becomes severe
B) When the cold departs
C) When water becomes warm
D) When the person travels to a warmer place

Key Takeaway
The principle 'ما جاز لعذر بطل بزواله' ensures that temporary dispensations do not outlive their cause, preserving the original ruling.

The Maxim of Necessity: Scope and Application
Applying the maxim of necessity to various fiqh rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'الضرورة تقدر بقدرها' to identify the precise scope of concessions in various fiqh rulings.

Core Idea
Necessity legitimizes the prohibited, but only to the exact extent required.

The Hanafi school uses the maxim of darurah (necessity) to permit otherwise prohibited acts when genuine need arises. However, this permission is strictly limited — it does not create a blanket exemption. This passage from al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir presents several furu' where scholars applied this rule, drawing fine distinctions between similar cases based on the presence or absence of necessity. Understanding these distinctions is essential for a mufti when issuing fatwas on concessions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Maxim
The passage begins with the principle that concessions are granted only out of necessity. State the maxim in Arabic: 'الضرورة تقدر بقدرها' (Necessity is measured by its extent).
This implies that the ruling changes only as much as needed to remove the hardship.
2
Classify Each Ruling
For each case mentioned (cat urine, sewage in wells, used water on clothes, martyr's blood, splints, physician's gaze, and the Shafi'i branch on marriage), identify the act and the default ruling before considering necessity.
E.g., cat urine is impure and normally must be removed from clothes and vessels.
3
Apply the Maxim
Explain how the maxim limits the concession: what is permitted (e.g., cat urine forgiven on clothes) and what remains prohibited (e.g., not on vessels because custom covers them, removing necessity).
Focus on the reasoning: 'li'l-darurah' or 'li-'adam al-darurah'.
4
Note Disagreements and Relied-Upon View
Some scholars differentiated between desert and city wells for dung. The text states the relied-upon view is no difference. Identify the mufta bihi position for each such variant.
The author uses 'al-mu'tamad' to indicate the operative view.
5
Synthesize the Mufti's Method
Formulate a two-step test: 1) Is there a genuine necessity that cannot be avoided? 2) Is the concession limited to the minimum required to remove that necessity? Apply this to a new case.
Example: A starving traveler may eat pork but only the amount to stay alive, not more.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
على سبيل الحاجة لأنه إنما أُبيح للضرورة. قال في الكنز : وينتفع فيها بعلف وطعام وحطب وسلاح ودهن بلا قسمة وبعد الخروج منها ،لا، وما فضل ردّ إلى الغنيمة . وأفتوا بالعفو عن بول السنور في الثياب دون الأواني، لأنه لا ضرورة في الأواني لجريان العادة بتخميرها. وفرَّق كثير من المشايخ في البعر بين آبار الفلوات؛ فيعفى عن قليله للضرورة لأنه ليس لها رؤوس حاجزة والإبل تبعر ،حولها، وبين آبار الأمصار لعدم الضرورة بخلاف الكثير ولكن المعتمد عدم الفرق بين آبار الفلوات والأمصار، وبين الصحيح والمنكسر وبين الرطب واليابس. ويعفى عن ثياب المتوضيء إذا أصابها من الماء المستعمل، وعلى رواية النجاسة للضرورة ولا يعفى عما يصيب ثوب غيره لعدمها، ودم الشهيد طاهر في حق نفسه، نجس في حق غيره لعدم الضرورة، والجبيرة يجب ألا تستر من الصحيح إلا بقدر ما لا بد منه، والطبيب إنما ينظر من العورة بقدر الحاجة. وفرع الشافعية عليها أن المجنون لا يجوز تزويجه أكثر من واحدة لاندفاع الحاجة بها. (انتهى). ولم أره لمشايخنا الله رحمهم.
Out of necessity, because it was only permitted for necessity. He said in al-Kanz: And one benefits from them (spoils) by using fodder, food, firewood, weapons, and oil without distribution, and after exiting from them, no, and what remains is returned to the spoils. And they gave fatwa of pardon regarding cat urine on clothes but not on vessels, because there is no necessity in vessels due to the custom of covering them. And many scholars differentiated in dung between wells of the deserts: its little is pardoned out of necessity because they have no barrier heads and camels dung around them, and between wells of cities due to lack of necessity, unlike much, but the relied-upon is no difference between desert wells and city wells, and between sound and broken, and between wet and dry. And the clothes of the one performing wudu are pardoned if they are affected by used water, and according to the narration of impurity for necessity, and what touches another's garment is not pardoned due to lack of necessity. And the blood of the martyr is pure in his own right, impure in the right of others due to lack of necessity. And the splint must not cover more of the healthy part than necessary. And the physician only looks at the private part to the extent of need. And the Shafi'is derived from it that a mad person may not be married to more than one wife because need is fulfilled by one. (End). And I did not see it from our scholars, may Allah have mercy on them.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a collection of applications of the Hanafi maxim of necessity (darurah). The text begins by stating that permission is granted only to the extent of need. It then cites from al-Kanz rulings on using spoils for fodder, food, etc., without distribution. Several specific concessions follow: cat urine is forgiven on clothes because custom covers vessels, so no necessity there. Dung in desert wells is forgiven in small amounts due to necessity, but the relied-upon view is no distinction between desert and city wells. Used water on a wudu-doer's clothes is forgiven on one narration, but not on another's clothes. A martyr's blood is pure for himself but impure for others. A splint covers only the needed area; a physician's gaze is limited. Finally, the Shafi'i branch that a mad person can only marry one wife due to sufficiency of need. The author notes he did not see this from Hanafi scholars.

Key Terms
ضرورة (darurah): Necessity that permits concessions in otherwise prohibited matters, evaluated objectively and limited to the extent required.
رخصة (rukhsah): A concession that permits what is normally prohibited due to necessity or hardship.
عفو (afw): Pardon of minor impurities due to difficulty in avoidance, often based on custom or necessity.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is cat urine forgiven on clothes but not on vessels according to the Hanafi maxim?
A) Because garments are less pure than vessels
B) Because there is necessity on clothes (cannot cover them) but no necessity on vessels (custom covers them)
C) Because vessels are made of metal and cat urine is more harmful
D) Because the ruling only applies to cats, not vessels

Key Takeaway
The maxim of necessity permits only what is required to remove the hardship, not more; the mufti must assess the scope of each case individually.

Sub-Maxim of Temporary Concessions: Removal of Excuse Ends the Ruling
Introducing and applying a sub-maxim of temporary concessions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'ما جاز لعذر بطل بزواله', identify its rank as a sub-maxim (sughra), and apply it to furuat in ibadat and shahadat.

Core Idea
Temporary excuses permit temporary concessions; when the excuse ends, the concession ends.

This lesson introduces a powerful sub-maxim that governs temporary legal concessions. It is closely related to the broader principle that hardship begets ease, but it has a razor-sharp edge: the moment the excuse disappears, the concession vanishes. Understanding this maxim is essential for a mufti to correctly apply rulings in changing circumstances.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim in Arabic and English
Read the maxim carefully: 'ما جاز لعذر بطل بزواله'. Understand that 'جاز لعذر' means 'permitted due to an excuse' and 'بطل بزواله' means 'becomes invalid with its removal'.
This is a dabt (restrictive sub-maxim) within the broader qa'ida 'المشقة تجلب التيسير'.
2
Identify the scope and rank
Classify the maxim as sughra (minor) and dabt (restrictive). Its scope covers temporary concessions in ibadat (especially tayammum) and shahadat (testimony upon testimony).
It does not apply to permanent exemptions, e.g., a chronic illness that never ends.
3
Work through the furuat from the text
Apply the maxim to the two furuat: (1) Tayammum becomes invalid when the excuse (loss of water, illness, cold) ends. (2) Secondary testimony becomes invalid when the original witness recovers from illness or returns from travel, because the excuse (death, illness, travel) ceases.
For tayammum, the ruling is that wudu becomes obligatory immediately. For testimony, the secondary testimony is no longer permissible.
4
Identify any istithna' (exceptions)
Check if there are exceptions where the maxim does not apply. In the source, no exceptions are listed, but note that this maxim is restricted by the broader principle 'الضرر يزال' (harm must be removed) in cases where removing the concession would cause greater harm.
For example, if a traveler has no water and is ill, tayammum is valid until both excuses end, but if one ends while the other remains, the maxim may not fully apply.
5
Consider related qawaid
Relate this maxim to others: 'الضرر يزال' restricts it (if harm remains, the concession may persist), and 'المشقة تجلب التيسير' is its broader umbrella.
Understanding these relationships helps a mufti choose the correct maxim in complex cases.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقرب من هذه القاعدة : ما جاز لعذر بطل بزواله فبطل التيمم إذا قدر على استعمال الماء. فإن كان لفَقْد الماء بطل بالقدرة عليه وإن كان لمرض بطل ببرئه، وإن كان لبرد بطل بزواله. وينبغي أن تخرج على هذه القاعدة الشهادة على الشهادة؛ إذا كان الأصل مريضًا فصح بعد الإشهاد أو مسافرًا فقدم أن يبطل الإشهاد على القول بأنها لا تجوز إلا لموت الأصل أو مرضه أو سفره .
It is close to this maxim: What is permitted due to an excuse becomes invalid with its removal. So tayammum becomes invalid when one is able to use water. If it was due to lack of water, it becomes invalid with ability upon it; if due to illness, invalid with recovery; if due to cold, invalid with its cessation. And one should derive from this maxim the testimony upon testimony: if the original witness was ill and became healthy after bearing witness, or was traveling and arrived, then the testimony becomes invalid according to the opinion that it is only allowed due to death, illness, or travel of the original.
Source pages p. 74

Commentary
This passage is a tadhneeb (appendix) that presents a subsidiary maxim: 'What is permitted due to an excuse becomes invalid when the excuse ceases.' The text applies it to tayammum: once water is available, tayammum ends, whether the excuse was lack of water, illness, or cold. Then it applies to testimony upon testimony (shahada ala shahada): if the original witness recovers from illness or returns from travel, the secondary testimony becomes invalid, because the excuse (death, illness, travel) that permitted it is gone. This is based on a Hanafi position.

Key Terms
عذر (udhr): A valid excuse that permits a legal concession

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'ما جاز لعذر بطل بزواله', when does tayammum become invalid?
A) Only when the person intentionally decides to use water
B) When the excuse (e.g., lack of water, illness, cold) ceases to exist
C) Only when the person recites the intention to end tayammum
D) Tayammum never becomes invalid once performed

Key Takeaway
A mufti must remember that temporary concessions are precisely that: temporary — when the excuse ends, the concession ends immediately.

The Maxim of Choosing the Lesser Harm and Its Core Applications
Maxim with multiple furu' applications

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal maxim of choosing the lesser harm to concrete scenarios across ibadat, mu'amalat, and jinayat, and identify when the sub-maxim 'if equal, choose' applies.

Core Idea
When two harms conflict, commit the lesser to avoid the greater; if equal, choose freely.

This lesson unpacks a cornerstone of Hanafi legal reasoning: the maxim of choosing the lesser harm. Rooted in the principle that necessity permits the forbidden only to the extent required, this maxim guides the mufti when no option is completely permissible. We will work through the maxim's statement, its sub-maxim for equal harms, and a series of practical applications from prayer, purity, and necessity, learning to weigh harms correctly.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its sub-maxim
Identify the main maxim 'when two harms conflict, the greater is observed by committing the lesser' and the sub-maxim 'if they are equal, choose freely'.
The sub-maxim is the dabt of the main qawida.
2
Apply the maxim to prayer cases
Analyze the examples of the wounded man, the elderly man, and the woman covering awrah. Determine which harm is greater and which is committed.
The weighing is based on legal consequences, not personal preference.
3
Apply the maxim to impurity cases
Work through the two-garment scenarios, noting the quarter-of-the-garment threshold. Understand when equality triggers choice, and when the greater harm dictates the lesser garment.
The quarter threshold is a Hanafi specific rule.
4
Apply the maxim to necessity and coercion
Consider the examples of carrion vs. another's property, and carrion vs. game for a muhrim. Identify which harm is greater and the resulted ruling.
Note the ikhtilaf within the school on some of these applications.

Source Text

(انتهى).
وبه
.
وذكر الزيلعي في آخر كتاب الإكراه لو قال له لتلقين نفسك في النار أو من الجبل أو لأقتلنّك ؛ وكان الإلقاء بحيث لا ينجو منه ولكن فيه نوع خفة فله الخيار، إن شاء فعل ذلك، وإن شاء لم يفعل وصبر حتى يقتل ؛ عند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله لأنه ابتلي ببليتين فيختار ما هو الأهون في زعمه وعندهما يصبر ولا يفعل ذلك، لأن مباشرة الفعل سعى في إهلاك نفسه فيصبر تحاميا عنه وأصله أن الحريق إذا وقع في سفينة وعلم أنه لو صبر فيها يحترق، ولو وقع في الماء يغرق؛ فعنده يختار أيهما شاء. وعندهما يصبر . ثم إذا ألقى نفسه في النار فاحترق فعلى المكره القصاص، بخلاف ما إذا قال له لتلقين نفسك من رأس الجبل أو لأقتلنّك بالسيف فألقى نفسه فمات. فعند أبي حنيفة رحمه الله تجب الدّية وهي مسألة القتل بالمثقل. (انتهى).
And the Shafi'is detailed: they said: If the owner of the animal is with it, he is negligent by failing to guard it. Then if it is not edible, the pot is broken and he is liable for the diminution in value; or if edible, there are two views on slaughtering it. And if he is not with it, then if the owner of the pot was negligent, it is broken and no compensation; otherwise, he gets compensation. And it is appropriate to append to the cow case the case of his dinar falling into another's inkwell and not being retrievable except by breaking it. [unclear] And among them: the permissibility of entering another's house if his property falls into it and he fears that if he asks its owner [he will not give it?]. And among them: the issue of obtaining by force what is owed to him. And among them: the permissibility of slitting the belly of a dead animal to extract the offspring if its life is hoped for. Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) ordered it and the offspring lived, as in al-Multaqit. They said: unlike if it swallowed a pearl and died, then its belly is not slit because the sanctity of a human is greater than the sanctity of property. The Shafi'is equated them in the permissibility of slitting. And in Tahdhib al-Qalanisi: forbiddance and permissibility, and the value of the pearl is in his estate; if he left nothing, nothing is owed. (End). And among them: the request for division by the majority owner while his partner is harmed; the majority owner is granted his request according to one view because his harm in not dividing is greater than his partner's harm from dividing. The fourth: arising from this principle is a fourth principle, which is: if two harms conflict, the greater harm is observed by committing the lesser. Al-Zayla'i said in the chapter on conditions of prayer: the principle in the genus of these issues is that whoever is afflicted with two trials and they are equal, he takes whichever he wishes; if they differ, he chooses the lighter one because direct commission of the forbidden is not permissible except by necessity, and there is no necessity with respect to [increase?]. The minor ritual impurity (hadath) (?) . Example: a man who has a wound; if he prostrates his wound flows, and if he does not prostrate it does not flow; he prays by gesturing for bowing and prostration because leaving prostration is lighter than praying with ritual impurity, even though leaving prostration is permissible voluntarily on a mount. But with impurity it is not permissible at all. Similarly, an old man who cannot recite standing but can recite sitting: he prays sitting because it is permissible voluntarily in supererogatory prayer, and leaving recitation is not permissible at all; if he prayed in both acts with impurity and left recitation, it is not valid. And if there were two garments, each with impurity more than a dirham, he chooses as long as neither reaches a quarter of the garment because they are equal in prevention. If the blood of one is a quarter and the other less, he prays in the one with less blood, and the opposite is not allowed because for a quarter the ruling of the whole applies. If each has a quarter and one has more but not reaching three quarters, and the other has a quarter, he prays in whichever he wishes because they are equal in ruling; but it is better to pray in the one with less impurity. If one is pure and the other has a quarter less than a quarter, he prays in the one whose quarter is pure, and the opposite is not allowed. If a woman praying standing would uncover part of her awrah that prevents the validity of prayer, and if she prays sitting nothing is uncovered, she prays sitting because leaving standing is lighter. If the garment covers a quarter of her head and she leaves covering the head, it is not permissible. If it covers less than a quarter, leaving it does not harm her because for a quarter the ruling of the whole applies, and less than that is not given the ruling of the whole; covering is better to minimize exposure. (End). And from this category is what is mentioned in al-Khulasa: if when he goes out to the congregation he cannot stand, but if he prays at home he can stand, he goes out and prays sitting, and that is correct. And a correction was transmitted from Sharh Munyat al-Musalli: he prays at home standing, and that is more apparent. And of the type: if one is compelled and has carrion and another's property, he eats the carrion. And from some of our companions (may Allah have mercy on them): whoever finds another's food, carrion is not permissible for him. And from Ibn Sama'a: usurpation is preferable to carrion. Al-Tahawi and others adopted it, and al-Karkhi gave him a choice, likewise in al-Bazzaziyya. And if a muhrim is compelled and has carrion and game, he eats carrion and not game according to the relied view. In al-Bazzaziyya: if the game is slaughtered, game is preferable by consensus. And if one is compelled and has game and another's property, game is preferable. Likewise, game is preferable to human flesh. And from Muhammad: game is preferable to pork flesh. (End). And al-Zayla'i mentioned at the end of the book on coercion: if someone says to him: 'Throw yourself into the fire or from the mountain or I will kill you,' and the throwing is such that he would not survive but there is some ease in it, he has the choice; if he wishes he does it, and if he wishes he does not and endures until he is killed, according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) because he is afflicted with two trials and chooses what is lighter in his estimation. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he endures and does not do it, because directly performing the action is striving to destroy himself, so he endures avoiding it. Its origin is: if a fire occurs on a ship and he knows that if he endures in it he will burn, and if he falls into the water he will drown; according to him, he chooses whichever he wishes. According to the two, he endures. Then if he throws himself into the fire and burns, the coercer is liable for retaliatory punishment (qisas); unlike if he said to him: 'Throw yourself from the top of the mountain or I will kill you with the sword,' and he threw himself and died; according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) blood money (diya) is due, and it is the issue of killing with a blunt object. (End).
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the legal maxim 'If two harms conflict, the greater harm is avoided by committing the lesser harm.' It belongs to the qawaid al-fiqhiyya and is a sub-maxim of the overarching principle 'Harm must be removed' (الضرر يزال). The text begins by reporting the Shafi'i view on a case where an animal breaks a pot, illustrating how negligence affects liability. The author then analogizes a coin falling into an inkwell. Next, he lists several applications: entering another's house to retrieve fallen property, forcibly taking what is owed, slitting a dead animal to save a live offspring (with a comparison between Abu Hanifa and Shafi'is on human versus property sanctity), and a majority partner forcing division despite the minority partner's harm. The core maxim is explicitly stated: when two harms conflict, the greater is observed by committing the lesser. Al-Zayla'i's principle from the chapter on prayer conditions is quoted: if afflicted with two equal trials, choose any; if unequal, choose the lighter, because direct commission of the forbidden is only allowed by necessity, and there is no necessity for the extra harm. Examples follow: a wounded person gestures in prayer rather than pray with impurity; an old man sits to recite; choosing between two impure garments based on the quarter-of-the-garment rule; a woman sits to cover her awrah; leaving home for congregation even if it means praying sitting. Further examples: eating carrion over another's property (with differing opinions), game vs. carrion for a muhrim, game vs. property, and finally the coercion case where a person is threatened to throw himself off a cliff or be killed. According to Abu Hanifa, he may choose the lighter option (the jump), while according to the Sahibayn, he must endure and not actively cause his own death. The ruling on qisas vs. diya follows from the nature of the coercer's action. All these applications demonstrate the practical operation of the maxim in various chapters of fiqh.

Key Terms
المفسدة (al-mafsada): Harm or detriment; in this context, a prohibited action or situation that causes harm
الضرر (al-ḍarar): Harm; the scale by which the greater and lesser harm are compared
الأهون (al-ahwan): The lighter or lesser (harm); the option to be chosen when two harms differ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the text, if a man has two impure garments, one with impurity covering slightly more than a quarter (but less than half) and the other with impurity covering exactly a quarter, what is the ruling?
A) He must pray in the one with less than a quarter because it is purer.
B) He may choose either because both are equal in legal effect.
C) He prays in the one with a quarter because it is the greater harm and must be avoided.
D) He must combine both garments or find a third.

Key Takeaway
The maxim guides the mufti to commit the lesser harm when two prohibitions are unavoidable, with free choice when harms are equal.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
advanced
10 min
The Coercion Exception: Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Throwing Oneself into Fire
Intra-Hanafi dispute on coercion and self-endangerment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi disagreement between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn on whether a coerced person may throw themselves into certain death, and apply the resulting fatwa on criminal liability.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa allows choosing the lesser harm; the Sahibayn require endurance because direct self-destruction is prohibited.

This lesson examines a classic intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf that tests the limits of the lesser harm maxim: when a person is coerced into throwing themselves from a cliff or into a fire. Abu Hanifa grants the victim a choice to take the easier death, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad forbid it. We'll explore the proofs, the thamarah al-khilaf in criminal liability, and the relied-upon position for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
State the issue and the positions
The coercer threatens death unless the victim jumps. Abu Hanifa says he may choose to jump; the Sahibayn say he must endure and not jump.
The case assumes jumping leads to certain death but with some 'lightness' (type of death).
2
Examine Abu Hanifa's proof
He analogizes to the ship fire case: a person on a burning ship may choose to jump into water (drowning) or stay (burn). Both are inevitable death; he chooses the lighter. Here, the victim is afflicted with two trials (painful death by sword vs. easier death by fire) and chooses the lighter.
Abu Hanifa's dalil is qiyas based on the ship fire case, which is itself a mas'ala from Zahir al-Riwayah.
3
Examine the Sahibayn's proof
They argue that directly performing the act (jumping) is seeking to destroy oneself, which is prohibited. The victim must endure and not actively bring about death. The ship fire case: they say the passenger should stay and not jump.
Their proof is the principle of not actively causing one's death, tied to the sanctity of life.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
If the victim jumps and dies, the coercer's liability differs: if into fire (death by fire), qisas; if from a mountain (death by fall), diya according to Abu Hanifa. According to the Sahibayn, liability may differ (not specified here).
The practical consequence is crucial for criminal cases.
5
Determine the mufta bihi
The text does not explicitly state which view is relied upon. Later Hanafi works (e.g., al-Hidayah) often prefer the view of the Sahibayn in matters of self-preservation. However, the maxim of choosing the lesser harm is still widely applied in other contexts.
The mufti should note the ikhtilaf and apply the majority or the position that best fits the situation, typically the Sahibayn's view to avoid encouraging suicide.

(انتهى).
And the Shafi'is detailed: they said: If the owner of the animal is with it, he is negligent by failing to guard it. Then if it is not edible, the pot is broken and he is liable for the diminution in value; or if edible, there are two views on slaughtering it. And if he is not with it, then if the owner of the pot was negligent, it is broken and no compensation; otherwise, he gets compensation. And it is appropriate to append to the cow case the case of his dinar falling into another's inkwell and not being retrievable except by breaking it. [unclear] And among them: the permissibility of entering another's house if his property falls into it and he fears that if he asks its owner [he will not give it?]. And among them: the issue of obtaining by force what is owed to him. And among them: the permissibility of slitting the belly of a dead animal to extract the offspring if its life is hoped for. Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) ordered it and the offspring lived, as in al-Multaqit. They said: unlike if it swallowed a pearl and died, then its belly is not slit because the sanctity of a human is greater than the sanctity of property. The Shafi'is equated them in the permissibility of slitting. And in Tahdhib al-Qalanisi: forbiddance and permissibility, and the value of the pearl is in his estate; if he left nothing, nothing is owed. (End). And among them: the request for division by the majority owner while his partner is harmed; the majority owner is granted his request according to one view because his harm in not dividing is greater than his partner's harm from dividing. The fourth: arising from this principle is a fourth principle, which is: if two harms conflict, the greater harm is observed by committing the lesser. Al-Zayla'i said in the chapter on conditions of prayer: the principle in the genus of these issues is that whoever is afflicted with two trials and they are equal, he takes whichever he wishes; if they differ, he chooses the lighter one because direct commission of the forbidden is not permissible except by necessity, and there is no necessity with respect to [increase?]. The minor ritual impurity (hadath) (?) . Example: a man who has a wound; if he prostrates his wound flows, and if he does not prostrate it does not flow; he prays by gesturing for bowing and prostration because leaving prostration is lighter than praying with ritual impurity, even though leaving prostration is permissible voluntarily on a mount. But with impurity it is not permissible at all. Similarly, an old man who cannot recite standing but can recite sitting: he prays sitting because it is permissible voluntarily in supererogatory prayer, and leaving recitation is not permissible at all; if he prayed in both acts with impurity and left recitation, it is not valid. And if there were two garments, each with impurity more than a dirham, he chooses as long as neither reaches a quarter of the garment because they are equal in prevention. If the blood of one is a quarter and the other less, he prays in the one with less blood, and the opposite is not allowed because for a quarter the ruling of the whole applies. If each has a quarter and one has more but not reaching three quarters, and the other has a quarter, he prays in whichever he wishes because they are equal in ruling; but it is better to pray in the one with less impurity. If one is pure and the other has a quarter less than a quarter, he prays in the one whose quarter is pure, and the opposite is not allowed. If a woman praying standing would uncover part of her awrah that prevents the validity of prayer, and if she prays sitting nothing is uncovered, she prays sitting because leaving standing is lighter. If the garment covers a quarter of her head and she leaves covering the head, it is not permissible. If it covers less than a quarter, leaving it does not harm her because for a quarter the ruling of the whole applies, and less than that is not given the ruling of the whole; covering is better to minimize exposure. (End). And from this category is what is mentioned in al-Khulasa: if when he goes out to the congregation he cannot stand, but if he prays at home he can stand, he goes out and prays sitting, and that is correct. And a correction was transmitted from Sharh Munyat al-Musalli: he prays at home standing, and that is more apparent. And of the type: if one is compelled and has carrion and another's property, he eats the carrion. And from some of our companions (may Allah have mercy on them): whoever finds another's food, carrion is not permissible for him. And from Ibn Sama'a: usurpation is preferable to carrion. Al-Tahawi and others adopted it, and al-Karkhi gave him a choice, likewise in al-Bazzaziyya. And if a muhrim is compelled and has carrion and game, he eats carrion and not game according to the relied view. In al-Bazzaziyya: if the game is slaughtered, game is preferable by consensus. And if one is compelled and has game and another's property, game is preferable. Likewise, game is preferable to human flesh. And from Muhammad: game is preferable to pork flesh. (End). And al-Zayla'i mentioned at the end of the book on coercion: if someone says to him: 'Throw yourself into the fire or from the mountain or I will kill you,' and the throwing is such that he would not survive but there is some ease in it, he has the choice; if he wishes he does it, and if he wishes he does not and endures until he is killed, according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) because he is afflicted with two trials and chooses what is lighter in his estimation. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), he endures and does not do it, because directly performing the action is striving to destroy himself, so he endures avoiding it. Its origin is: if a fire occurs on a ship and he knows that if he endures in it he will burn, and if he falls into the water he will drown; according to him, he chooses whichever he wishes. According to the two, he endures. Then if he throws himself into the fire and burns, the coercer is liable for retaliatory punishment (qisas); unlike if he said to him: 'Throw yourself from the top of the mountain or I will kill you with the sword,' and he threw himself and died; according to Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him) blood money (diya) is due, and it is the issue of killing with a blunt object. (End).
Source pages p. 76

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the legal maxim 'If two harms conflict, the greater harm is avoided by committing the lesser harm.' It belongs to the qawaid al-fiqhiyya and is a sub-maxim of the overarching principle 'Harm must be removed' (الضرر يزال). The text begins by reporting the Shafi'i view on a case where an animal breaks a pot, illustrating how negligence affects liability. The author then analogizes a coin falling into an inkwell. Next, he lists several applications: entering another's house to retrieve fallen property, forcibly taking what is owed, slitting a dead animal to save a live offspring (with a comparison between Abu Hanifa and Shafi'is on human versus property sanctity), and a majority partner forcing division despite the minority partner's harm. The core maxim is explicitly stated: when two harms conflict, the greater is observed by committing the lesser. Al-Zayla'i's principle from the chapter on prayer conditions is quoted: if afflicted with two equal trials, choose any; if unequal, choose the lighter, because direct commission of the forbidden is only allowed by necessity, and there is no necessity for the extra harm. Examples follow: a wounded person gestures in prayer rather than pray with impurity; an old man sits to recite; choosing between two impure garments based on the quarter-of-the-garment rule; a woman sits to cover her awrah; leaving home for congregation even if it means praying sitting. Further examples: eating carrion over another's property (with differing opinions), game vs. carrion for a muhrim, game vs. property, and finally the coercion case where a person is threatened to throw himself off a cliff or be killed. According to Abu Hanifa, he may choose the lighter option (the jump), while according to the Sahibayn, he must endure and not actively cause his own death. The ruling on qisas vs. diya follows from the nature of the coercer's action. All these applications demonstrate the practical operation of the maxim in various chapters of fiqh.

Key Terms
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): Coercion (duress) that overcomes a person's free will, affecting legal rulings.
تلف النفس (talaf an-nafs): Self-destruction; in this context, actively causing one's own death.
القصاص (al-qiṣāṣ): Retaliation in kind (life for life) for intentional killing.
الدية (ad-diya): Blood money paid to the victim's heirs in cases of unintentional killing or when qisas is not applicable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Sahibayn, why must a coerced person not throw themselves from a cliff even if the alternative is a more painful death?
A) Because jumping is not a sure death.
B) Because directly performing the act is actively destroying oneself, which is prohibited.
C) Because the cliff is higher than the ship.
D) Because the coercion is not considered severe enough.

Key Takeaway
The Sahibayn's view restricts the lesser harm maxim: when the choice involves actively ending one's own life, endurance, not action, is required.

Need Takes the Place of Necessity: The Sub-Maxim and Its Applications
Sixth sub-maxim: need as a basis for legal concessions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the sub-maxim 'al-haja tunazzilu manzilat al-darura' to identify which need-based concessions are permitted in Hanafi fiqh and distinguish genuine need from mere convenience.

Core Idea
Need, whether general or specific, justifies exceptions to standard legal rules in transactions.

This lesson examines a key legal maxim that bridges necessity (darura) and ordinary need (haja). While darura permits outright prohibitions, haja permits concessions in transactions that would otherwise be invalid due to uncertainty or other defects. The author presents several applications from various chapters of fiqh, showing how the Hanafi school uses this maxim to facilitate economic life.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Identify the Arabic text 'الحاجة تنزل منزلة الضرورة' and note its rank as a wusta (intermediate) maxim governing primarily mu'amalat.
The maxim is kulliya (universal) and parallels 'al-darurat tubihu al-mahzurat'.
2
Classify the type of need
Understand that need can be 'amma (general) or khassa (specific); both can justify concessions. Recognize that need must be genuine, not a mere desire.
The text distinguishes general economic needs from individual whims.
3
Analyse the furuat (applications)
For each example (ijarah, salam, istisna', bay' al-wafa, etc.), identify the usual prohibition (e.g., gharar, bay' ma'dum) and how need overrides it to permit the contract.
Look at the specific illa (cause) that would normally invalidate and how need removes it.
4
Check the istithna' (exception)
Note that when need is absent (e.g., exchanging benefits of houses of the same type), the maxim does not apply and the original rule stands.
This exception reinforces that need is the operative cause for the concession.
5
Relate to other maxims
Compare with 'al-darurat tubihu al-mahzurat' (necessity permits prohibitions) and 'al-mashaqqa tujlib al-taysir' (hardship brings ease). Understand how haja is a lower threshold than darura.
The Hanafi school uses these maxims together to create a flexible system.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السادسة : الحاجة تنزل منزلة الضرورة عامة كانت أو خاصة، ولهذا جوزت الإجارة على خلاف القياس للحاجة ولذا قلنا لا تجوز إجارة بيت بمنافع بيت لاتحاد جنس فلا حاجة بخلاف ما إذا اختلف. ومنها ضمان الدرك جوز على خلاف القياس. ومن ذلك جواز السلم على خلاف القياس لكونه بيع المعدوم دفعًا لحاجة المفاليس. ومنها جواز الاستصناع للحاجة ودخول الحمام مع جهالة مكثه فيها وما يستعمله من مائها وشربة السقاء ومنها الإفتاء بصحة بيع الوفاء حين كثر الدين على أهل بخارى وهكذا بمصر، وقد سموه بيع الأمانة، والشافعية يسمونه الرهن المُعاد، وهكذا سماه به في الملتقط. وقد ذكرناه في شرح الكنز من باب خيار الشرط. وفي القنية والبغية يجوز للمحتاج الاستقراض بالربح. (انتهى).
The sixth: Need descends to the level of necessity, whether general or specific. For this reason, ijarah (hire) was permitted contrary to analogy due to need. Hence we say: renting a house in exchange for the benefits of another house is not permitted because of unity of category, so there is no need, unlike when categories differ. Among its applications: daman al-darak (guarantee of recourse) was permitted contrary to analogy. From that is the permissibility of salam (forward sale) contrary to analogy because it is selling a non-existent object to meet the need of the needy. Among its applications: permissibility of istisna' (manufacturing contract) due to need, and entering a public bath despite ignorance of the duration and the amount of water used, and the drink of the waterskin. And from it is the fatwa on the validity of bay' al-wafa (sale with right of repurchase) when debt became widespread among the people of Bukhara and likewise in Egypt; they called it bay' al-amanah, and the Shafi'is call it al-rahn al-mu'ad, and thus it was named in al-Multaqat. We mentioned it in Sharh al-Kanz in the chapter of khiyar al-shart. In al-Qunyah and al-Bughyah: it is permissible for a needy person to borrow with profit (interest) out of need.
Source pages p. 79

Commentary
This passage introduces the sub-maxim: 'Need takes the place of necessity.' The author explains that both general and specific needs can justify legal concessions that would otherwise be prohibited or invalid by analogy. Several examples from mu'amalat are given: ijarah (hiring) is allowed despite uncertainty because of general need; however, exchanging benefits of houses of the same type is not allowed because there is no real need, but if the types differ, need arises. Other applications include daman al-darak (a guarantee that covers the seller's liability), salam (advance payment for future delivery) which is a sale of a non-existent object, istisna' (manufacturing contract) which involves uncertainty, and entering a public bath where the amount of water used is unknown. The most notable application is bay' al-wafa (a sale with the right of repurchase), which was permitted in Bukhara and Egypt due to widespread debt; it is also known as 'bay' al-amanah' and, by Shafi'is, 'al-rahn al-mu'ad'. The passage ends by noting that a needy person may borrow with profit (i.e., pay interest) out of necessity – a clear indication that need can permit what is normally prohibited.

Key Terms
حاجة (haja): Need, a lesser degree of necessity that still justifies concessions
ضرورة (darura): Necessity, the highest level of emergency that permits prohibitions
بيع الوفاء (bay' al-wafa): A sale with the condition that the seller can repurchase upon returning the price

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is salam (forward sale) permitted despite being a sale of a non-existent object?
A) Because it is explicitly allowed in the Quran
B) Because of the need of the needy (mufalis)
C) Because it is a form of ijarah
D) Because it is a kind of gift

Key Takeaway
Need, when genuine and widespread, can justify exceptions to normal legal rules in financial transactions.

Establishing Custom: One, Two, or Three Occurrences?
Explains the condition for custom to be legally effective, with disagreement among Hanafi imams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions for establishing custom (al-‘ādah) in Hanafi law, distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa, Muhammad, and Abu Yusuf, and apply the mufta bihi position to menstrual habit and dog training cases.

Core Idea
Custom is authoritative, but its establishment requires repetition — one occurrence for menstruation (Abu Yusuf's view), three for dog training.

The legal maxim 'al-‘ādah muḥakkamah' (custom is authoritative) is foundational in Hanafi fiqh. But when does a practice become a 'custom' that carries legal weight? This passage, from Ibn Nujaym's Al-Ashbāh wa al-Naẓā'ir, examines two concrete cases — a woman's menstrual habit and a hunting dog's training — to illustrate the disagreement among Hanafi imams on the number of repetitions needed to establish a custom. Understanding this helps a mufti determine which opinion is operative for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the governing maxim
The passage begins by connecting to the base maxim 'al-‘ādah muḥakkamah'. Recognize that this discussion is a sub-issue of that maxim.
The maxim itself is not quoted, but the context makes it clear.
2
Parse the two branches
Two examples are given: (1) a woman's menstrual habit in the chapter of hayd, and (2) a hunting dog's training habit. Note the different thresholds.
Menstrual custom: disagreement over one vs. two occurrences. Dog training: three occurrences.
3
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute
For menstrual custom, Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require two occurrences; Abu Yusuf accepts one. Consider the rationale: one occurrence may be accidental, but custom can also be shaped by a single instance if it becomes a pattern.
This is an example of ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school (intra-madhhab).
4
Determine the mufta bihi
The text states 'wa `alayhi al-fatwa' — the fatwa is according to Abu Yusuf's view. This is the operative position for issuing verdicts.
The later Hanafi scholars adopted Abu Yusuf's view for practical convenience.
5
Apply to the dog training case
For a hunting dog to be considered trained, the habit of leaving prey uneaten is established after three instances. This is a different threshold and is not disputed here.
The number three is a distinct rule for this specific context.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتتعلق بهذه القاعدة مباحث :
المبحث الأول :
بماذا تثبت العادة؟ وفي ذلك فروع:
1 - العادة في باب الحيض ؛ اختلف فيها فعند أبي حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله لا تثبت إلا بمرتين، وعند أبي يوسف رحمه الله تثبت بمرة واحدة، قالوا وعليه الفتوى، وهل الخلاف في الأصلية أو في الجعلية أو فيهما؟ مستوفى في الخلاصة وغيرها .
2 - تعليم الكلب الصائد بترك أكله للصيد بأضر يصير الترك عادة وذلك بترك الأكل ثلاث مرات.
(۱) الظئر الناقة تعطف على ولد غيرها ومنه قيل للمرأة الأجنبية التي تحضن ولد غيرها ظئر. وللرجل الحاضن أيضًا، ويجمع على على أظار .
And connected to this principle are discussions: First discussion: By what is custom established? Therein are branches: 1 - Custom in the chapter of menstruation; there is disagreement about it. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, may Allah have mercy on them, it is not established except by two times, and according to Abu Yusuf, may Allah have mercy on him, it is established by one time. They said: and upon it is the fatwa. And is the disagreement in the original (custom) or in the derived (habitual) or in both? It is covered fully in al-Khulasa and others. 2 - Teaching the hunting dog by leaving its prey uneaten: the leaving becomes a habit by leaving the eating three times. (1) Al-Za’ir: the she-camel that shows affection to the young of another; hence a foreign woman who nurses the child of another is called za’ir, and also the male guardian, plural: az’ār.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This is a sub-discussion under the maxim 'Custom is authoritative', addressing how custom is established. The author presents two branches: First, in the context of menstruation (establishing a woman's habit), the Hanafi imams differ: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require the occurrence twice for it to be considered a custom, while Abu Yusuf deems one occurrence sufficient. The fatwa is according to Abu Yusuf. There is further nuance whether the disagreement is in the original custom or in the derived habitual ruling. Second, for teaching a hunting dog to be considered trained, the habit of leaving prey uneaten is established by three instances of not eating, not just once or twice. These examples show that the number of repetitions required to establish a custom varies by context and is subject to scholarly interpretation.

Key Terms
العادة (al-‘ādah): Custom or habitual practice; in this passage, specifically a woman's menstrual habit or a dog's training habit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the relied-upon position for establishing a woman's menstrual custom?
A) It requires three occurrences.
B) It requires two occurrences.
C) It requires one occurrence.
D) It requires four occurrences.

Key Takeaway
A custom is not established arbitrarily; for menstrual habits, one occurrence suffices per the mufta bihi, while for dog training, three occurrences are required — demonstrating that context determines the threshold.
