How Custom Is Established: Three Branches
First discussion on how custom is proven

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim on custom to different fiqh branches, distinguishing the required number of occurrences for menstruation, dog training, and gifting to a judge.

Core Idea
Custom is established by recurrence; the number of occurrences varies by context and madhhab authority.

This passage opens the discussion on how custom is legally proven. It presents three branches (furu‘) where the establishment of custom is debated. For menstruation, there is a well-known ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad and Abu Yusuf. For dog training, three instances are specified. For gifting to a judge, the author admits uncertainty. This lesson parses these branches and links them to the broader qawida.

Guided Path
1
State the qawida on custom
Identify the general maxim: ‘Custom is established by recurrence’ (al-‘ādah tuthbatu bi al-takrār). This is a sub-maxim (sughra) under the major maxim al-‘ādah muhakkamah.
The passage does not quote the maxim explicitly but applies it.
2
Analyze the menstruation branch
Note the ikhtilaf between Abu Hanifa/Muhammad (requires two occurrences) and Abu Yusuf (one occurrence). Identify which view is mufta bihi (Abu Yusuf's) and understand the thamarah.
The dispute also touches on original custom vs. derived custom.
3
Analyze the dog training branch
The passage states that a hunting dog becomes trained if it leaves eating the game three times. This establishes a custom of leaving. The number three is derived from practice, not the dispute.
This is an application where custom is set by a specific number in fiqh.
4
Analyze the gifting branch
The author admits he has not seen how the custom of gifting to a judge (which necessitates acceptance) is established. This shows that not all customs have clear proof; some remain unresolved.
This serves as a caution for the mufti: not every alleged custom is legally proven.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الأول : بماذا تثبت العادة؟ وفي ذلك فروع: ۱ - العادة في باب الحيض ; اختلف فيها فعند أبي حنيفة ومحمد رحمهما الله لا تثبت إلا بمرتين، وعند أبي يوسف رحمه الله تثبت بمرة واحدة، قالوا وعليه الفتوى، وهل الخلاف في الأصلية أو في الجعلية أو فيهما؟ مستوفى في الخلاصة وغيرها . ۲ - تعليم الكلب الصائد بترك أكله للصيد بأضر يصير الترك عادة وذلك بترك الأكل ثلاث مرات. ٣ - لم أر بماذا تثبت العادة بالإهداء للقاضي المقتضية للقبول.
First discussion: By what is custom established? And therein are branches: 1 - Custom in the chapter of menstruation: there is disagreement. According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on them), it is not established except by two occurrences. According to Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him), it is established by one occurrence. They said: and the fatwa is upon it. Is the disagreement regarding the original custom or derived or both? Detailed in al-Khulasah and others. 2 - Training a hunting dog to leave eating the hunt: [repeated] leaving becomes customary by leaving eating three times. 3 - I have not seen by what the custom of gifting to the judge that necessitates acceptance is established.
Source pages p. 80

Commentary
This passage is the first discussion (mabahith) that follows the general applications of the custom rule. It addresses: How is custom legally proven? The author gives three branches. The first is the custom of a woman's menstruation cycle. This is a critical issue for rulings related to menstruation (e.g., when does a woman become pure). Dispute: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require two occurrences for a pattern to be considered a custom; Abu Yusuf considers one occurrence sufficient. The fatwa is reportedly on the view of Abu Yusuf, though some detail the controversy between original custom ('urf asli) and incidental custom ('urf jali). The second branch is training a hunting dog: the dog becomes considered trained (taught) if it leaves eating the game three times; this three-time repetition establishes the custom of leaving. The third branch is a note by the author: he states that he has not found how the custom of gifting to a judge (which would justify acceptance) is established—this issue remains unresolved in the text. The passage thus highlights that not all customs are easily proven; some require specific evidentiary standards.

Key Terms
ثبوت العادة (thubūt al-'ādah): The legal recognition that a certain practice has become customary and therefore can be relied upon in rulings.
العادة الأصلية (al-'ādah al-aṣliyyah): The original custom of a woman's menstrual cycle (e.g., six days every month).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Once
B) Twice
C) Three times
D) Five times

Key Takeaway
Custom is established by recurrence, but the required number of occurrences varies by fiqh branch and madhhab authority; the mufta bihi differs from the dominant teaching in the menstruation case.

Custom Must Be Constant or Prevalent
Explains the requirement that custom must be prevalent or constant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qa'ida 'Custom is only considered if constant or prevalent' to identify which customs are legally binding and which are not.

Core Idea
A rare custom is no custom at all — only constant or prevalent practices shape contracts.

This lesson introduces the foundational Hanafi qa'ida that governs the legal recognition of custom ('urf). Not every local practice becomes part of a contract; the custom must be either constant (muṭṭarid) or overwhelmingly prevalent (ghālib). Through several furuat from sales and hiring, you will see how this rule operates in practice, and why a mufti must first verify the prevalence of a claimed custom before applying it.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its rank
Read the maxim in Arabic: إنما تعتبر العادة إذا اطّردت أو غلبت. Identify it as a wusta (intermediate) qa'ida of the kulliya (universal) type. Understand the two key criteria: constancy and prevalence.
Constancy (iṭṭirād) means the custom is consistently practiced; prevalence (ghalaba) means it is practiced by the majority.
2
Identify the scope
This qa'ida applies to all contracts and dealings where custom can determine unexpressed terms, such as sales (buyu), hiring (ijarah), and similar transactions.
It complements the companion qa'ida: 'The known by custom is like the stipulated condition.'
3
Work through the furuat from the text
For each furu' (sub-case) cited, apply the qa'ida: check if the custom is constant or prevalent. For example, a city with multiple currencies: the most prevalent currency is the basis. For weekly installments: the prevalent custom of weekly payments is binding even if not written.
The passage also includes cases of hiring a scribe, tailor, kohl-applyer, slave, animal, and wet nurse — in each, the ruling follows the prevalent custom.
4
Identify the complementary qa'ida
Note the companion qa'ida: 'The known by custom is like the stipulated condition.' This maxim reinforces that once a custom is proven as constant or prevalent, it functions as an implied term.
Together, these two qawa'id form the backbone of Hanafi 'urf theory.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المبحث الثاني: إنما تعتبر العادة إذا اطّردت أو غلبت؛ ولذا قالوا في البيع لو باع بدراهم أو دنانير وكانا في بلد اختلف فيه النقود مع الاختلاف في المالية والرواج انصرف البيع إلى الأغلب قال في الهداية : لأنه هو المتعارف فينصرف المطلق إليه. ومنها لو باع التاجر في السوق شيئًا بثمن ولم يصرحا بحلول ولا تأجيل وكان المتعارف فيما بينهم أن البائع يأخذ كل جمعة قدرًا معلوما انصرف إليه بلا بيان قالوا لأن المعروف كالمشروط ولكن إذا باعه المشتري تولية ولم يبين التقسيط للمشتري هل يكون للمشتري الخيار؟ فمنهم من أثبته الجمهور على أنه يبيعه مرابحة بلا بيان لكونه حالاً بالعقد، ذكره الزيلعي في التولية. ومنها في استئجار الكاتب قالوا الحبر عليه والأقلام والخياط قالوا الخيط هذا والإبرة عليه عملاً بالعرف وينبغي أن يكون الكحل على الكحال للعرف، ومن القبيل طعام العبد فإنه على المستأجر بخلاف علف الدابَّة فإنه على المؤجر حتى لو شرط على المستأجر فسدَّت كما في البزازية بخلاف استئجار الظئر بطعامها وكسوتها فإنه جائز وإن كان مجهولاً للعرف.
Second discussion: Custom is only considered if it is constant or prevalent. Hence they said in sale: If someone sells for dirhams or dinars, and in that city there are differing currencies with differences in value and circulation, the sale is referred to the most prevalent. Al-Hidayah said: Because it is the customary, so the absolute is directed to it. And among them: If a merchant sells something in the market for a price and they did not specify immediate payment or deferment, and the custom among them is that the seller takes a known amount every Friday, the contract is referred to that without clarification. They said: because the known is like the stipulated. But if the buyer sells it as a commission (tawliyah) and does not clarify the installment plan, does the buyer have an option? Some affirmed it, the majority that he sells it as murabahah without clarification because it is considered cash upon contract, mentioned by al-Zayla'i in al-Tawliyah. And among them: in hiring a scribe: they said the ink is upon him (the hirer), and the pen; and for a tailor: they said the thread and the needle are upon him, acting on custom. And similarly, kohl should be upon the kohl-applyer, by custom. And from this type is the food of the slave: it is upon the hirer, unlike the fodder of the animal, which is upon the owner, so that if he stipulates it is upon the hirer, the contract becomes corrupt, as in al-Bazzaziyyah, unlike hiring a wet nurse with her food and clothing: it is valid even if unspecified, due to custom.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This is the second discussion of the rule on custom. The condition for a custom to be legally recognized is that it must be constant (mutṭarid) or prevalent (ghālib). The author then enumerates several furuat. The first is a sale in a city with multiple currencies: the payment is interpreted as the most prevalent currency, because the absolute term (unqualified) is directed to what is customary. The second is a sale with deferred payment by custom: if the custom is to pay weekly installments, the contract is valid without explicit stipulation, because the known (al-ma'rūf) is like the stipulated condition. However, if the buyer resells on tawliyah (sale at cost) without disclosing the installment terms, there is a difference of opinion: some give the second buyer an option; the majority hold that the first seller had a cash sale, so the resale is murabahah (cost-plus) without mentioning installments. Then the passage covers hiring of professionals: for a scribe, ink and pen are on the hirer; for a tailor, thread and needle; for a kohl-applyer, kohl—all by custom. For hiring a slave, food is on the hirer; for an animal, fodder is on the owner (employer). If they stipulate fodder on the hirer, the contract is invalid. But for a wet nurse, her food and clothing are on the hirer even if unspecified, because custom so dictates.

Key Terms
اطراد (iṭṭirād): Constancy of a custom, meaning it is consistently practiced without exception.
غلب (ghalab): Prevalence: the custom is practiced by the majority of the community.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi qa'ida, when is a custom legally considered?
A) When it is written down.
B) When it is constant or prevalent.
C) When the parties agree on it explicitly.
D) When it is ancient.

Key Takeaway
A custom is legally binding in Hanafi fiqh only if it is constant (everyone follows it) or prevalent (the vast majority follows it); rare practices have no legal effect.

Custom in Hire: Furuat from Animal Fodder to Holidays
Applications of custom principle in ijarah, waqf, and employment cases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim of custom (ʿurf) to determine liability and salary entitlement in hire, waqf, and employment cases, distinguishing stipulation from default custom.

Core Idea
Custom determines who bears costs and whether salary is due on off-days, unless a contract stipulates otherwise.

This lesson examines four practical applications of the Hanafi principle of custom (ʿurf) in hire, waqf, and employment. Through concrete cases — animal fodder, mosque candles, school holidays, and judge's vacation — you will see how custom fills gaps left unaddressed by explicit contract terms. The lesson also highlights the boundary between custom and stipulation, preparing you to apply this maxim in modern fatwa scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the governing maxim
Recognize that custom (ʿurf) is the operative principle. The maxim 'al-ʿurf fi bab al-ijarah wa nahwiha' means custom governs hire-like transactions.
This is a sub-maxim (ḍābiṭ) from the larger qawāʿid framework.
2
Examine the first furuʻ: animal fodder
The owner of the animal is responsible for fodder by custom; the hirer is not liable if the animal starves. Understand the logic: the custom determines who bears maintenance costs.
Contrast with explicit contract terms, which would override custom.
3
Examine the second furuʻ: mosque candles
The imam/muʼadhdhin cannot take leftover candle wax without explicit donor permission, unless local custom permits it. See how custom can substitute for explicit permission.
Custom must be localized and established; general practice is insufficient.
4
Examine the third furuʻ: school holidays
If teacher holidays are stipulated, no salary deduction. Otherwise, the rule for judges' vacation applies by analogy.
The passage indicates this is less clear but links to the judge's case.
5
Examine the fourth furuʻ: judge's vacation
The more correct view (al-aṣaḥḥ) is that the judge receives salary on vacation because the day does not break continuity of entitlement. This ruling then extends to teachers by analogy.
The authority is al-Munyah and Ibn Wahban; the contrary view (qīl) is weaker.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وتفرّع على أن علف الدابة على مالكها دون المستأجر أن المستأجر لو تركها بلا علف حتى ماتت جوعًا لم يضمن كما في البزازية. ومنها ما في وقف القنية : بعث شمعًا في شهر رمضان إلى مسجد فاحترق وبقي منه ثلثه أو دونه ليس للإمام ولا للمؤذن أن يأخذه بغير إذن الدافع، ولو كان العرف في ذلك الموضع أن الإمام أو المؤذن يأخذه من غير صريح الإذن في ذلك كان له ذلك . (انتهى). ومنها البطالة في المدارس كأيام الأعياد ويوم عاشوراء وشهر رمضان في درس الفقه لم أرها صريحة في كلامهم والمسألة على وجهين فإن كانت مشروطة لم يسقط من المعلوم شيء وإلا فينبغي أن يلحق ببطالة القاضي. وقد أخذوا في أخذ القاضي ما رتب من بيت المال في يوم بطالته، فقال في المحيط إنه يأخذ في يوم البطالة لأنه يستريح لليوم الثاني وقيل لا يأخذ. (انتهى). وفي المنية : القاضي يستحق الكفاية من بيت المال في يوم البطالة في الأصح واختاره في منظومة ابن وهبان وقال إنه الأظهر فينبغي أن يكون كذلك في المدارس لأن يوم البطالة لا يقطع الأجرة.
And it is derived from this that the fodder of the animal is upon its owner, not the hirer: if the hirer leaves it without fodder until it dies of hunger, he does not guarantee it, as in al-Bazzaziyyah. And among them is what is in Waqf al-Qunyah: Someone sent candles in Ramadan to a mosque, and it burned, and one-third or less remained. Neither the imam nor the mu'adhdhin may take it without the permission of the donor. If the custom in that place is that the imam or mu'adhdhin takes it without explicit permission, then he may do so. (End). And among them is the vacation in schools, like the days of Eid, Ashura, and Ramadan in the lesson of fiqh. I have not seen it explicitly in their words, and the matter has two aspects: if the vacation is stipulated, nothing is deducted from the salary; otherwise, it should be attached to the vacation of the judge. And they have discussed a judge taking his stipulated salary from the Bayt al-Mal on his vacation day: In al-Muhit, it is said that he takes it because he rests for the next day; and it is said he does not take it. (End). And in al-Munyah: The judge deserves his sufficiency from the Bayt al-Mal on the vacation day in the more correct view, and Ibn Wahban chose it in his poem, saying it is most apparent. So it should be likewise for schools, because the vacation day does not break the salary.
Source pages p. 81

Commentary
This passage continues the applications of the custom principle, branching from the earlier point about animal fodder. It applies to four cases: (1) If a hirer of an animal leaves it unfed and it dies, he is not liable because fodder was the owner's responsibility. (2) Regarding candles sent to a mosque as charity: if they burn and remnants remain, the imam or mu'adhdhin cannot take them without donor's permission — unless custom allows it, in which case it is permissible. (3) Regarding school holidays (e.g., for Eid, Ashura, Ramadan) for a fiqh teacher: if the holidays are stipulated in the contract, no deduction from salary; otherwise, it should follow the rule of judges' vacation. (4) For judges: there is a difference of opinion whether they receive salary from the public treasury on vacation days. The stronger view (al-Munyah) is that they do receive it because the vacation does not break entitlement. By analogy, teachers should also receive their salary on holidays.

Key Terms
البطالة (al-baṭālah): Vacation or non-working days in the context of employees (teachers, judges).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a judge takes a vacation day, what is the more correct view regarding his salary from the Bayt al-Mal?
A) He does not receive any salary for that day.
B) He receives his salary because the vacation does not break entitlement.
C) He receives half salary.
D) He must make up the day.

Key Takeaway
Custom determines the financial obligations in hire and salary arrangements unless the contract explicitly stipulates otherwise.

Two Applications of Custom: Imam's Leave and Waqf Interpretation
Two applications of custom: imam's leave and waqf interpretation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qā‘idah 'al-ma‘rūf ‘urfan ka al-mashrūṭ shartan' to determine the permissibility of an imam's leave and to interpret an ambiguous waqf condition in light of custom.

Core Idea
Custom gives rulings the force of explicit conditions.

This lesson presents two concrete fā'idahs (benefits) from al-Ashbāh wa al-Naẓā'ir that demonstrate the qā‘idah 'al-ma‘rūf ‘urfan ka al-mashrūṭ shartan' in action. The first case concerns an imam taking a weekly leave from leading prayers; the second deals with schools endowed for hadith study where the waqf founder's intent is unclear. In both, custom clarifies what is legally permissible and defines the scope of obligations.

Guided Path
1
Identify the governing qā‘idah
Recognise that the maxim 'al-ma‘rūf ‘urfan ka al-mashrūṭ shartan' (What is known by custom is like what is stipulated as a condition) is the legal basis underlying both cases.
This is a wusṭā (intermediate) maxim governing many muʿāmalāt.
2
Analyse the first fur‘: Imam’s leave
The imam leaving for a week per month for rest or family visit is tolerated by custom; therefore, it is as if it were explicitly stipulated in his contract. Custom makes it permissible.
Source: al-Qunyah, chapter of imāmah.
3
Analyse the second fur‘: Waqf for hadith
When the waqf condition is ambiguous, the custom of the country determines how the subject (hadith study) is to be implemented. In Syria, one method; in Egypt, another. Custom interprets the condition.
Ibn Ḥajar al-ʿAsqalānī’s fatwā, cited by al-Suyūṭī.
4
Derive the muftā bihī ruling
The muftī should apply the custom of the time and place to resolve the ambiguity. No single method is mandated beyond what custom establishes.
This illustrates the flexibility of Ḥanafī fatwā through qawā‘id.
5
Note the complementary qā‘idah
The maxim 'innamā tu‘tabar al-‘ādah idhā iṭṭaradat aw ghalabat' (Custom is only considered if it is consistent or predominant) limits the application – the custom must be well-established.
This prevents reliance on rare or temporary practices.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فائدة :
(1)
نقل في القنية أن الإمام للمسجد يسامح في كل شهر أسبوعًا للاستراحة أو لزيارة أهله وعبارته في باب الإمامة : إمام يترك الإمامة لزيارة أقربائه في الرساتيق أسبوعًا أو نحوه أو لمصيبته أو لاستراحته لا بأس به ومثله عفو في العادة والشرع.
(انتهى) .
ومنها المدارس الموقوفة على درس الحديث ولا يعلم مراد الواقف فيها هل يدرس علم الحديث الذي هو معرفة المصطلح كمختصر ابن الصلاح، أو يقرأ متن الحديث كالبخاري ومسلم ونحوهما ويتكلم على ما في الحديث من فقه أو عربية أو لغة أو مشكل أو اختلاف كما هو عُرْف الناس الآن؟ قال الجلال الأسيوطي: وهو شرط المدرسة الشيخونية كما رأيته في شرط واقفها قال وقد سأل شيخ الإسلام أبو الفضل ابن حجر شيخه الحافظ أبا الفضل العراقي عن ذلك، فأجاب بأن الظاهر اتباع شروط الواقفين فإنهم يختلفون في الشروط، وكذلك اصطلاح كل بلد؛ فإن أهل الشام يلقون دروس الحديث بالسماع ويتكلم المدرّس في بعض الأوقات، بخلاف المصريين فإن العادة جرت بينهم في هذه الأعصار بالجمع بين الأمرين بحسب ما يقرأ فيها من الحديث .
Benefit: (1) It is transmitted in al-Qunyah that the imam of the mosque is excused each month for a week for rest or visiting his family. His wording in the chapter of Imamate: An imam who leaves the imamate to visit his relatives in the countryside for a week or so, or due to a calamity, or for rest, there is no harm in it, and similar is forgiven by custom and law. (End). Among them: schools endowed for the study of hadith, and the intent of the waqf is not known: is it to study the science of hadith which is knowledge of terminology like Mukhtasar Ibn al-Salah, or to recite the text of hadith like Bukhari and Muslim and the like, and discuss the fiqh, Arabic, language, problems, or differences as is the custom of people now? Al-Jalal al-Asyuti said: This is the condition of the Shaykhuniyyah school as I saw in the condition of its waqf. He said: Shaykh al-Islam Abu al-Fadl ibn Hajar asked his teacher al-Hafiz Abu al-Fadl al-Iraqi about that, and he answered: The apparent is to follow the conditions of the waqf founders, for they differ in conditions, and similarly the custom of each country; for the people of Syria give hadith lessons by listening and the teacher speaks at some times, unlike the Egyptians, for custom has prevailed among them in these times to combine both matters according to what is read of hadith.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This fā'idah (benefit) discusses two separate issues where custom plays a role. First, it is permissible for the imam of a mosque to take a week off per month for rest or visiting family, based on what is tolerated by custom and law. The source is al-Qunyah. Second, the issue of schools endowed for hadith study: the intent of the waqf is interpreted according to the custom of the people. The text mentions that in Syria, the custom is to teach hadith with listening and occasional commentary, while in Egypt the custom combines both recitation and commentary. The response from Ibn Hajar emphasizes following the conditions of the waqf and the custom of the country.

Key Terms
العادة (al-'ādah): Custom; established practice.
شرط الواقف (shart al-wāqif): Condition set by the founder of a waqf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
How did al-Jalal al-Asyuti resolve the ambiguity in the waqf for hadith study?
A) He mandated a specific curriculum.
B) He followed the custom of the country.
C) He relied solely on the literal text of the waqf.
D) He declared the waqf invalid.

Key Takeaway
Custom functions as an implicit condition, allowing flexibility in rulings without violating contractual or waqf terms.

Custom Prevails in Oaths: 'Urf al-Istimal
Rule: custom prevails in oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qawida 'urf al-istimal to determine the non-breach of oaths in cases of conflict between custom and literal scriptural usage.

Core Idea
In Hanafi oaths, common usage overrides literal Quranic metaphors: custom defines meaning.

This passage from Al-Ashbah wa al-Naza'ir introduces a crucial principle for muftis: when interpreting oaths, the customary meaning of a term among people takes precedence over any metaphorical or literal usage found in the Quran or Sunnah. The examples provided—bed vs. ground, lamp vs. sun—demonstrate that a person's oath is judged by what the community ordinarily understands, not by scriptural poetry. As a mufti, your task is to determine the 'urf al-istimal in your time and place.

Guided Path
1
State the qawida
Identify the exact maxim: 'يقدّم عرف الاستعمال في الأيمان' ('Custom of common usage is given precedence in oaths'). This is the core rule.
Memorize the Arabic.
2
Understand the conflict
There is a potential conflict between the literal/scriptural usage of a term (e.g., Quran calls earth a 'firash' and sun a 'siraj') and the common usage among people. In oaths, which one governs?
The Quranic usage is metaphorical, not meant for legal definitions.
3
Apply the qawida
The qawida states that in oaths, common usage (urf al-istimal) prevails. So if someone swears by a term, the meaning is determined by what people commonly understand, not by the Quranic metaphor.
This is specific to oaths (ayman); other contexts may differ.
4
Work through the furuat
Apply to the given cases: (1) Swearing not to sit on a 'firash' (bed) → sitting on ground does not break the oath because common usage does not call ground a bed. (2) Swearing not to use a 'siraj' (lamp) → using sunlight does not break the oath because common usage does not call sunlight a lamp.
The result is consistent: the oath is not broken.
5
Note the scope
This qawida applies primarily in the chapter of oaths (ayman). It is a sughra (minor) maxim that aids muftis in resolving ambiguity. It is also connected to the broader principle 'العبرة في الأيمان بالعرف' (consideration in oaths is given to custom).
In other fiqh chapters, different rules may apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في تعارض العُزف مع الشرع :
فإذا تعارضا قدم عُرْف الاستعمال خصوصا في الأيمان، فإذا حلف لا يجلس على الفراش أو على البساط أو لا يستضيء بالسراج لم يحنث بجلوسه على الأرض ولا بالاستضاءة بالشمس وإن سماها الله تعالى فراشًا وبساطا وسمى الشمس سراجا.
Chapter on conflict between custom and shariah: When they conflict, the custom of common usage is given precedence, especially in oaths. So if someone swears not to sit on a bed (firash) or on a carpet (bisat) or not to use a lamp (siraj), he does not break his oath by sitting on the ground or by using sunlight, even though Allah Almighty called the earth a bed and a carpet and called the sun a lamp.
Source pages p. 82

Commentary
This short subsection addresses a key principle: when there is a conflict between the literal meaning used in the Quran or Sunnah and the common usage of a term, the common usage is given precedence, especially in the context of oaths. The example given is that a person who swears not to sit on a 'firash' (bed) does not break his oath by sitting on the ground, because in common usage, 'firash' does not apply to the ground, even though the Quran metaphorically calls the earth a 'firash'. Similarly, swearing not to use a 'siraj' (lamp) does not include using sunlight, even though the sun is called a 'siraj' in the Quran. This illustrates that in oaths, the intent is determined by the common understanding of the people, not by metaphorical or literal scriptural usage.

Key Terms
عرف الاستعمال ('urf al-istimal): Common usage; the customary meaning of a term among people
اليمين (al-yamin): Oath; a sworn statement

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person swears not to sit on a 'firash' (bed), and then sits on the ground, does he break his oath?
A) Yes, because the Quran calls the earth a firash
B) No, because custom does not call the ground a firash
C) Yes, if he intended the literal meaning
D) No, because grounds cannot be sat on

Key Takeaway
When custom and scriptural metaphor clash in oaths, the mufti follows the people's usage, not the poet's license.

The Maxim: Oaths Are Built on Custom, Not Linguistic Realities
Elaboration of the principle with multiple examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim 'al-ayman mabniyyah 'ala al-urf' to determine the scope of an oath and evaluate the resulting fatwa.

Core Idea
Oaths are interpreted by local custom, not dictionary definitions.

In this passage, we encounter a key Hanafi legal maxim that governs the entire chapter of oaths (ayman). The principle states that when a person swears an oath, the terms of that oath are understood according to the custom of the swearer's locality, not according to their literal linguistic meanings. The author, following Imam al-Zayla'i, then elaborates several practical branches (furu') that illustrate the operation of this maxim. As a mufti, understanding this maxim is essential for issuing correct fatwas in oath-related cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core maxim
Extract the principle from the opening statement: al-ayman mabniyyah 'ala al-urf. Understand its scope.
2
Examine the furuat
For each branch, identify the oath term and the customary interpretation in the given locality. Note the ruling: does the oath break or not?
3
Analyze the pattern
Recognize how custom overrides linguistic reality in each example. Consider what would happen if custom were not considered.
4
Apply to a new scenario
Given a new oath and a new locality, determine what would break the oath based on local custom.
5
Identify exceptions and boundaries
Note that some terms (like qata'if) require intention, and that places of worship are not 'houses'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في تعارض العُرف اللغة :- صرّح الزيلعي وغيره بأن الأيمان مبنيَّة على العُرف لا على الحقائق اللغوية وعليها فروع: منها: لو حلف لا يأكل الخبز حنث بما يعتاده أهل بلده، ففي القاهرة لا يحنث إلا بخبز البر وفي طبرستان ينصرف إلى خبز الأرز، وفي زبيد إلى خبز الذرة والدخن، ولو أكل الحالف خلاف ما عندهم من الخبز لم يحنث ولا يحنث بأكل القطائف إلا بالنية . ومنها : الشّواء والطبيخ على اللحم، فلا يحنث بالباذنجان والجزر المشوي، ولا يحنث بالمزورة في الطبيخ ولا بالأرز المطبوخ بالسمن بخلاف المطبوخ بالدهن ولا بقلية يابسة . تنبيه: ومنها الرأس ما يُباع في مصره فلا يحنث إلا برأس الغنم. ومنها حلف لا يدخل بيتا فدخل بيعة أو كنيسة أو بيت نار أو الكعبة لم يحنث.
Chapter on the conflict of custom and language: Al-Zayla'i and others explicitly stated that oaths are built on custom, not on linguistic realities. Upon this [principle] are branches: Among them: If he swore not to eat bread, he breaks his oath by what is customary among the people of his town. In Cairo he does not break his oath except by wheat bread, in Tabaristan it refers to rice bread, and in Zabid to maize and millet bread. If the swearer ate something other than the bread customary among them, he does not break his oath. He does not break his oath by eating qata'if (pancakes) except with intention. Among them: [oaths regarding] grilled and cooked [food] that is [customarily] meat: he does not break his oath by grilled eggplant and carrots, nor by 'muzawwara' in cooking, nor by rice cooked with ghee as opposed to that cooked with oil, nor by dry qaliya (fried food). A note: Among them [branches]: The head: what is sold in his city — he only breaks his oath by a sheep's head. Among them: If he swore not to enter a house and he entered a church, a synagogue, a fire temple, or the Kaaba, he does not break his oath.
Source pages p. 83

Commentary
This passage is a chapter (fasl) dedicated to elaborating the conflict between custom and language in the context of oaths. It begins by citing the authority of al-Zayla'i who affirms the foundational principle: oaths are based on custom, not on the linguistic realities of words. Several practical branches illustrate this: (1) An oath not to eat 'bread' is broken by what is customary in the swearer's locale—wheat bread in Cairo, rice bread in Tabaristan, millet bread in Zabid. Eating a non-customary type does not break the oath. Qata'if (pancakes) require explicit intention to be included. (2) For oaths not to eat 'grilled' or 'cooked' that are customarily meat, the oath is not broken by vegetables prepared in those ways (e.g., grilled eggplant, cooked carrots, rice cooked with ghee), because the custom is that these terms refer to meat dishes. A note adds: (3) An oath not to eat 'head' refers only to the type of head sold in that locality (e.g., sheep's head). (4) An oath not to enter a 'house' is not broken by entering a church, synagogue, fire temple, or the Kaaba, because custom restricts 'house' to ordinary dwellings.

Key Terms
عرف (urf): Customary usage and common understanding of a community.
حقيقة لغوية (haqiqah lughawiyyah): The literal or lexical meaning of a word as defined by Arabic language.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if a man in Cairo swears not to eat 'bread', which of the following would break his oath?
A) Eating any type of bread.
B) Eating only wheat bread (khubz al-burr).
C) Eating rice bread (khubz al-aruzz).
D) Eating qata'if (pancakes).

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, oaths are interpreted according to the local custom of the swearer, overriding the literal linguistic meanings of terms.

When Literal Meaning Prevails Over Custom in Oaths
Four exceptions to the rule that oaths are based on custom

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that literal meaning governs oaths unless literal application is impossible, distinguishing cases where custom does not override.

Core Idea
Custom overrides literal meaning in oaths only when literal application is impossible.

The general principle is that oaths are built on custom (عرف). However, this passage presents four exceptional cases where the literal meaning is followed instead. These cases reveal a deeper rule: when the literal meaning of a word can be applied practically, custom does not restrict it. The passage also records a debate among Hanafi scholars on the validity of practical custom (عرف عملي) as a restrictor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four exceptional cases
Read the passage and list the four scenarios where an oath is interpreted literally despite contradictory custom. Note the differing scholarly opinions.
Case 1: 'meat' includes pork/human; Case 2: 'animal' includes human; Case 3: 'house' includes spider's house for destruction but not entry; Case 4: 'meat' includes liver/tripe according to Kufan custom.
2
Analyze the legal reasoning behind each exception
For each case, determine why the literal meaning is applied. Take note of the distinctions made by al-Zayla'i and Ibn al-Humam.
E.g., in Case 3, the difference between 'destroy a house' and 'enter a house': literal application is possible for destruction but not entry.
3
Examine the debate on practical custom
Focus on the first and second cases where al-Zayla'i argues that practical custom does not restrict, but Ibn al-Humam refutes this. Understand the reasoning: habit (عادة) is itself practical custom.
The key quote: 'الحقيقية تترك بدلالة العادة' — literal meaning is abandoned by indication of habit.
4
Derive the mufta bihi and its implications
Based on the passage, what is the operative ruling for fatwa? Note that custom of the swearer (including non-Arab customs) is considered, following the fourth case.
The passage concludes that the mufti should consider local custom for liver/tripe and for non-Arab lands.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
خرجت عن بناء الأيمان على العُرف مسائل : الأولى : حلف لا يأكل لحما حنث بأكل لحم الخنزير والآدمي على ما في الكنز ولكن الفتوى على خلافه وجواب الزيلعي بأنه عُرف عملي فلا يصلح مقيدًا بخلاف العُرف اللفظي فقد ردَّه في فتح القدير بقولهم في الأصول: الحقيقية تترك بدلالة العادة إذ ليست العادة إلا عُرفًا عمليا . (انتهى) . الثانية : حلف لا يركب حيوانا يحنث بالركوب على الإنسان لتناول اللفظ، والعُرف العملي وهو أنه لا يركب عادة لا يصلح مقيَّدا ذكره الزيلعي . بخلاف لا يركب دابة كما قدمناه. وقد استمر على ما مهده وقد علمت رده لكن لم يجب ابن الهمام عن هذا الفرع. مع أنه الثالثة : لو حلف لا يهدم بيتا حنث بهدم بيت العنكبوت، بخلاف لا يدخل بيتا وفرَّق الزيلعي بينهما بإمكان العمل بحقيقته في الهدم بخلاف الدخول ولو صح هذا الملك لم يصح بناء الأيمان على العُرف إلا عند تعذر العمل بحقيقته اللغوية. الرابعة : حلف لا يأكل لحمًا حنث بأكل الكبد والكرش على ما في الكنز لحمّا عُرفًا، ولذا قال في المحيط إنه إنما يحنث على عادة أهل الكوفة وأما عُرفنا فلا يحنث لأنه لا يُعَدُّ لحمّا. (انتهى). وهو حسن جدا، ومن هذا وأمثاله علم أن العجمي يعتبر عُرفه قطعا ومن هنا قال الزيلعي في قول صاحب الكنز : والواقف على السطح داخل إن المختار ألأ يحنث في العجم لأنه لا يسمى عندهم. (انتهى).
Cases that depart from building oaths on custom: First: If he swore not to eat meat, he breaks his oath by eating pork and human meat according to al-Kanz, but the fatwa is contrary to it. Al-Zayla'i's response that it is a practical custom and thus not suitable as a restrictor, unlike verbal custom, was refuted in Fath al-Qadir by their statement in usul: 'The [linguistic] reality is abandoned by indication of habit, since habit is nothing but practical custom.' (End). Second: If he swore not to ride an animal, he breaks his oath by riding a human because the word encompasses it, and practical custom—that he does not usually ride—is not a valid restrictor, as mentioned by al-Zayla'i. Unlike 'he does not ride a riding-beast' as we preceded. He insisted on what he laid down, and you have known its refutation, but Ibn al-Hammam did not respond to this branch. Despite that... Third: If he swore not to destroy a house, he breaks his oath by destroying a spider's house, unlike [the case of] not entering a house. Al-Zayla'i differentiated between them by the possibility of applying the [word's] literal meaning in destruction unlike entering. If this ownership is valid, then building oaths on custom is not valid except when literal application is impossible. Fourth: If he swore not to eat meat, he breaks his oath by eating liver and tripe according to al-Kanz because they are considered meat by custom. Hence it is said in al-Muhit that he only breaks his oath according to the custom of the people of Kufa; as for our custom, he does not break it because it is not counted as meat. (End). This is very good. From this and its likes, it is known that the non-Arab's custom is definitely considered. Hence al-Zayla'i said regarding the statement of the author of al-Kanz: 'And the one standing on the roof is inside'—the preferred [view] is that he does not break his oath in non-Arab [lands] because it is not called [inside] among them. (End).
Source pages p. 84

Commentary
This passage provides four cases where the principle that oaths are based on custom is set aside, i.e., the literal meaning takes precedence. The first case: an oath not to eat meat includes pork and human flesh according to al-Kanz, even though custom might exclude them. However, the fatwa (reliable ruling) is contrary, because practical custom (urf amali) is considered insufficient to restrict the literal meaning, as argued by al-Zayla'i, but this is refuted by Ibn al-Humam (in Fath al-Qadir) who argues that habit (adah) is itself a form of practical custom and can override the literal meaning. The second case: an oath not to ride an 'animal' includes riding a human, because the word 'hayawan' linguistically includes humans. The fact that people do not usually ride humans (practical custom) does not restrict the general word. This differs from 'dabbah' (riding-beast) which by custom excludes humans. The third case: an oath not to destroy a 'house' includes destroying a spider's web (called 'bayt' in Arabic), unlike an oath not to enter a 'house' which does not include such minimal structures. The difference is that literal application is possible for destruction (a spider's web can be 'destroyed') but not for entry (one cannot 'enter' a web). If this reasoning holds, then custom only applies when literal application is impossible. The fourth case: liver and tripe are considered 'meat' by Kufan custom but not by other customs; thus the ruling depends on the swearer's custom. This leads to the important conclusion that a non-Arab's custom is definitively considered. Al-Zayla'i extends this to the example of a man standing on a roof not being considered 'inside' in non-Arab lands.

Key Terms
عرف عملي (urf amali): Practical custom: habitual practice that is widespread, not verbal usage.
عرف لفظي (urf lafzi): Verbal custom: customary usage of a word in a particular meaning.
بيت العنكبوت (bayt al-ankabut): Spider's house/web. In Arabic, 'bayt' can mean a dwelling or a web.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the difference between an oath not to 'destroy a house' and not to 'enter a house'?
A) Destroying requires a larger structure; entering requires a door.
B) The literal meaning of 'house' can be applied to small structures in destruction but not in entry.
C) Custom overrides in destruction but not in entry.
D) There is no difference; both are treated the same.

Key Takeaway
Literal meaning governs oaths when literal application is possible; custom only overrides when literal application is impossible.

The Maxim: Customary Practice is Like a Stipulation
Discussion of the legal maxim that customary practice equals an explicit stipulation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim ‘al-ma‘rūf ‘urfan ka al-mashrūṭ shar‘an’ to identify when custom functions as an implied stipulation in Hanafi fiqh, and recognize its exceptions.

Core Idea
Custom can substitute for an explicit stipulation, but only where no contrary text or principle blocks it.

This lesson unpacks one of the most practical maxims in Hanafi mu‘amalat: customary practice (al-‘ādah al-muṭṭaridah) can take the place of an explicit condition. But how far does this principle extend? The classical Hanafi authorities debated its limits across various contracts, from tailoring to loans. By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply this maxim accurately, noting both its powerful reach and its clear exceptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its scope
State the Arabic maxim and its English meaning. Recognise that it is a specific application (dabt) of the greater maxim ‘al-‘ādah muḥakkamah’ and governs most transactional chapters.
The maxim is also phrased as ‘al-mashrūṭ ‘urfan ka al-mashrūṭ shar‘an’.
2
Examine the ikhtilaf among early Hanafis
Work through the dispute on the craftsman’s wage: Abu Hanifa rejects implied wage due to jahālah; Abu Yusuf conditions regularity of dealing; Muhammad considers the custom of the trade. Note that the fatwa follows Muhammad.
Al-Zayla‘ī’s tarjīḥ is the relied‑upon position.
3
Apply the maxim to the furū‘ in the text
For each case listed (khan, bathhouse, brokerage, loan with extra return, quarter for combatant, kitchen pottery, ‘āriyah with stipulation, father’s trousseau), explain how custom supplies the missing term.
Pay attention to how the custom must be ‘muṭṭaridah’ – consistent and prevailing.
4
Recognise the exceptions
Identify that wadī‘ah (deposit) and al-‘ayn al-mu’jarah (leased object) are never guaranteed even if custom suggests otherwise. The maxim does not override these explicit textual exclusions.
The governing principle here is ‘al-aṣl barā’at al-dhimmah’ (presumption of non‑liability).

Source Text

المبحث الثالث : العادة المطردة هل تنزل منزلة الشرط؟ قال في إجارة الظهيرية : والمعروف عُرْفًا كالمشروط شرعًا. (انتهى) وقالوا في الإجارات لو دفع ثوبًا إلى خياط ليخيطه له أو إلى صباغ ليصبغه له ولم يعين له أجرة، ثم اختلفا في الأخر وعدمه وقد جرت العادة بالعمل بالأجرة ؛ فهل ينزل منزلة شرط الأجرة؟ فيه اختلاف قال الإمام الأعظم : لا أجرة له، وقال أبو يوسف رحمه الله : إن كان الصابغ حريفًا له أي معاملاً له فله الأخر وإلا لا. وقال محمد رحمه الله : إن كان الصابغ معروفًا بهذه الصنعة بالأجر وقيام حاله بها كان القول قوله وإلا فلا اعتبارًا للظاهر المعتاد. وقال الزيلعي : والفتوى على قول محمد رحمه الله . (انتهى). ولا خصوصية لصابغ بل كل صانع نصب نفسه للعمل بأجرة فإن السكوت كالاشتراط . ومن هذا القبيل نزول الخان ودخول الحمام والدلال كما في البزازية. هذا القبيل المعدّ للاستغلال كما في الملتقط . ولذا قالوا المعروف كالمشروط، فعلى المفتى به صارت عادته كالمشروط صريحًا. ومن وهنا مسألتان لم أرهما الآن يمكن تخريجهما على أن المعروف كالمشروط، وفي البزازية المشروط عُرْفًا كالمشروط شرعًا منها لو جرت عادة المقترض بردّ أزيد مما اقترض هل يحرم إقراضه تنزيلاً لعادته بمنزلة الشرط؟ ومنها لو بارز كافر مسلما واطردت العادة بالأمان للكافر، هل يكون بمنزلة اشتراط الأمان له فيحرم على المسلمين إعانة المسلم عليه؟ وحين تأليف هذا المحل ورد علي سؤال فيمن أجر مطبحًا لطبخ السكر وفيه فخار، أذِنَ للمستأجر في استعمالها فتلف ذلك وقد جرى العُزف في المطابخ بضمانها على المستأجر، فأجبت بأن المعروف كالمشروط فصار كأنه صرح بضمانها عليه. والعارية إذا اشترط فيها الضمان على المستعير تصير مضمونة عندنا في رواية، ذكره الزيلعي في العارية وجزم به في الجوهرة ولم يقل في رواية لكن نقل بعده فرع البزازية عن الينابيع ثم قال : وأما الوديعة والعين المؤجرة فلا يضمنان بحال . (اهـ). ولكن في البزازية قال : أعرني هذا على أنه إن ضاع فأنا ضامن له فأعاره فضاع لم يضمن . (اهـ). ومما تفرع على أن المعروف كالمشروط لو جهز الأب بنته جهازا ودفعه لها ثم ادعى أنه عارية ولا بيّنة. ففيه اختلاف؛ والفتوى أنه إن كان العُزف مستمرًا أن الأب يدفع ذلك الجهاز ملكًا لا عارية لم يقبل قوله، وإن كان العُزف مشتركا فالقول للأب؛ كذا في شرح منظومة ابن وهبان وقال قاضي خان وعندي أن الأب إن كان من كرام الناس وأشرافهم لم يقبل قوله، وإن كان من أوساط الناس كان القول
Third Section: Does prevailing custom take the place of a stipulation? It was said in Ijarat al-Dhahiriyyah: 'What is known by custom is like that which is stipulated by law.' (End). And they said in the book of Ijarat: If someone gives a cloth to a tailor to sew or to a dyer to dye without specifying a wage, then they dispute regarding the wage and its absence, and the custom is to work for a wage; does this custom take the place of stipulating the wage? There is disagreement: Imam al-A'zam said: He has no wage. Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him) said: If the dyer is his regular customer, i.e., one who deals with him, then he has the wage; otherwise no. Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him) said: If the dyer is known for this craft with a wage and his livelihood depends on it, then his word is accepted; otherwise not, because consideration is given to the apparent custom. Al-Zayla'i said: The fatwa is according to Muhammad's statement. (End). There is no specificity to the dyer; rather every craftsman who sets himself up for work for a wage, silence is like a stipulation. Of this kind are staying at a khan, entering a bathhouse, and brokerage as in al-Bazzaziyyah. This category (is) prepared for exploitation as in al-Multaqat. Hence they said: 'What is known is like what is stipulated.' According to the mufta bihi, his custom became like an explicit stipulation. From this there are two issues I have not seen now that can be derived from the principle that what is known is like what is stipulated. In al-Bazzaziyyah: 'What is stipulated by custom is like what is stipulated by law.' Among them: If the borrower's custom is to return more than what he borrowed, is it prohibited to lend to him because his custom takes the place of a stipulation? And among them: If a disbeliever comes out against a Muslim and the custom of giving quarter to the disbeliever is prevalent, does that take the place of stipulating quarter for him so that it is prohibited for Muslims to assist the Muslim against him? At the time of compiling this section, a question came to me regarding someone who rented a kitchen for cooking sugar, and there was pottery in it; he permitted the tenant to use it, and it was destroyed, and the custom in kitchens is that the tenant is liable for it. I answered that what is known by custom is like what is stipulated, so it is as if he explicitly made him liable for it. Regarding a loan (ariyah): if liability is stipulated on the borrower, it becomes guaranteed according to us in one narration, as al-Zayla'i mentioned in al-Ariyah, and he affirmed it in al-Jawharah without saying 'in one narration', but after that he transmitted a branch from al-Bazzaziyyah from al-Yanabi' and then said: 'As for a deposit (wadi'ah) and a leased object (al-ayn al-mu'jara), they are not guaranteed under any circumstances.' (End). But in al-Bazzaziyyah it is said: 'Lend me this on the condition that if it is lost I am guarantor for it,' so he lent it and it was lost, and he did not guarantee it. (End). Among the branches derived from 'what is known is like what is stipulated': if a father equips his daughter with a trousseau and gives it to her, then claims it was a loan and there is no evidence, there is disagreement. The fatwa is that if the custom is continuous that the father gives the trousseau as ownership not a loan, his statement is not accepted; if the custom is mixed, the father's word is taken. Thus in Sharh Manzumah Ibn Wahban. Qadi Khan said: In my view, if the father is from the noble and honorable people, his statement is not accepted; if he is from the middle class, his word is accepted.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of the legal maxim 'المعروف عرفًا كالمشروط شرعًا' (What is known by custom is like that which is stipulated by law). It begins by questioning whether prevailing custom can take the place of an explicit stipulation in contracts. The text cites al-Dhahiriyyah's Ijarah stating the maxim. Then it presents a dispute among the early Hanafi authorities (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) regarding a craftsman who works without an explicit wage agreement: Abu Hanifa says no wage; Abu Yusuf distinguishes based on regular dealing; Muhammad considers the custom of the trade. Al-Zayla'i says the fatwa is with Muhammad. The ruling extends to all craftsmen. Examples include inns, bathhouses, and brokerage where silence implies payment. Two novel issues (loan with extra return, quarter for a combatant) are mentioned. The author then answers a question about a rented kitchen with pottery: custom of tenant liability makes it binding. Then the discussion of ariyah (loan) where stipulation of liability is generally effective, but wadi'ah and ijarah are exceptions. Finally, a family law example: a father's gift of a trousseau is presumed ownership if that is customary; otherwise, his word is accepted. Qadi Khan adds a distinction based on social class.

Key Terms
العادة المطردة (al-‘ādah al-muṭṭaridah): A consistent and prevailing custom that is recognized as authoritative in legal rulings
المعروف عرفًا كالمشروط شرعًا (al-ma‘rūf ‘urfan ka al-mashrūṭ shar‘an): The legal maxim: what is established by custom is legally equivalent to an explicit stipulation
القول قوله (al-qawl qawluhu): His statement is accepted (as evidence) due to the presumption of his credibility in the absence of contrary proof

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the fatwa regarding a dyer who dyes cloth without a specified wage, and then disputes the wage?
A) He gets no wage according to all scholars.
B) He gets the wage only if he is a regular customer of the owner.
C) He gets his customary wage if he is known to work for wages, according to Muhammad's view which is the fatwa.
D) He gets the wage only if he explicitly stipulated it.

Key Takeaway
Custom, when consistent and widespread, can substitute for an explicit stipulation, but this maxim does not override the categorical exclusion of wadī‘ah and mu’jar from liability.
